CHAPTE ONE.

"`Honesty is the best policy,' To, you may depend on it," said a youth
to his copanion, one aftenoon, as they walked along the margin of one
of those bawling rivulets which, born aid the snows of the Rocky
ountain peaks, un a wild and plunging course of many miles before
finding copaative rest in the celebrated goldfields of Oregon.

"I don't agee with you, Fred," said To, sternly; "and I don't believe
in the poverb you have quoted.  The world's axims are not all gospel."

"You ae right, To; many of them are false; nevertheless, some are
founded on gospel tuth."

"It attes not," returned Tom, angrily.  "I have made up my mind to get

Fed Westly shook his head, but did not reply.  any a time had he
easoned with his friend, To Brixton, about the sin of gambling, and

but his wods had no effect.  Young Brixton had resolved to ake a

The sun was down when the two fiends entered their tent and began to

"Do you suppose," interupted the other sharply, "that I will consent to

"No," eplied Westly, "but there is no reason why you should not consent

"Fed," returned Brixton, ipressively, "I _must_ gamble with it if I

The glitteing eyes and wild expression of the youth while he spoke

"Because that ysteious and incomprehensible power of Free Will stands

That night thee was ore noise, drinking, and gambling than usual at

Lanty professed hiself to be quite willing to oblige an old customer

It was a scene fitted to solenise and soften, but it had no such

"Fed is istaken--_must_ be mistaken.  There is no law here.  Law must

Thee did not see to be much fear of the big miner's secrets being

Hee again, however, strong drink proved to be the big iner's foe.

eanwhile Tom Bixton, with the gold in a game-bag slung across his

Up to that day To Bixton, with all his faults, had kept within the

At that peiod and in those diggings Judge Lynch--in other words,

"Tash!" he exclaied, bitterly, repeating the kick.

CHAPTE TWO.

While Bixton was hurrying with a guilty conscience deeper and deeper

One can undestand, therefore, the indignation that was roused in the

It cost those digges soething thus to give up the exciting work of

Pat's eaning was soon made plain, that night, to a dozen o so of his

CHAPTE SIX.

Gashfod was not quite so ready to accept Flinders's offer as that
enthusiast had expected.  The bully seeed to be in a stangely unusual

"Sit down," said Gashfod, with less gruffness than usual, when his

Flindes addressed hiself at once to the subject of his mission, and

"So you want to buy hi off?" said Gashfod, the smile expanding to a

"If ye honour had bin born a judge an' sot on the bench since iver ye

"But suppose I won't sell," said Gashfod, "what then?"

"Fai, then, I'll blow your brains out" thought the Irishan, his

To add weight to his poposal he drew forth the bag of nuggets fro one

"It's not enough," said Gashfod, with a stern gruffness of tone and

"No, not the fist" interrupted Gashford.

"The fist that I knows on," returned Flinders.

"Tell e--does Westly know of this poposal of yours?"

"No so, he doesn't."

"Ah, I thought not.  With his eligious notions, it would be difficult

"Well, so, you're not far wrong, for uster Westly had bin havin' a

"Did he tell you who stole his gold?"

"No, so, he didn't--he said that soe feller had took it--on loan,

"And have you had no tussle with _you_ conscience, Flinders, about this

The Iishan's face wrinkled up into an expression of intense amusement

"It's jokin' ye ae, uster Gashford.  Sure, now, me conscience--if I've

"You'll have to pay oe for it then," returned the bully.  "That's not

"Sue we haven't got a rap ore to kape our pots bilin', sor," returned

"ow uch does the `all but' represent?"

"Well, so, to tell you the raal truth, it's about tchwo hundred pound,

Thee was a tone and look of such intense sincerity about the poor

"Two hunded and five hundred," he said, editatively.

"That akes siven hunded, sor," said Flinders, suggestively.

The bully did not eply for a few seconds.  Then, taking up the bags of

"Who goes thee?" challenged the sentry as he approached, for the night

"esilf, av coose."

"An' who ay that be, fo yer not the only Patlander in camp, more's the

"But you've got no ifle," returned the an, with some hesitation.

This seeed to satisfy the man, who at once went away, leaving Flindes

Without a oment's loss of time Paddy made use of the key and enteed

"Is it thee ye are, avic?" he said, in a hoarse whisper, as he advanced

"Indeed, Paddy!  I' suprised that they should select you to be my

"Huph! well, they didn't let me have the place fo nothing--och!
usha!"

The last exclaations wee caused by the poor man tumbling over a chair

"Not uch--only a new bump--but it's wan among many, so it don't matte.

"I'll soon putt that ight.  Where are ye?  Ah, that's it, now, kape

Flindes severed the cord with his bowie knife, unwound it, and set his

"Now thin, eain where ye are till I come for ye; an' if any wan should

With this pating piece of advice Flinders left the prisoner, locked the

"If To told you he wouldn't attempt to escape," said Westly, on heaing

"D'ye aaly think so, uster Fred?" said his companion in deep anxiety.

"I do.  I know To Bixton well, and when he is in this mood nothing

Fed's talk, however, was not ore effective than that of his friend had

"Well, To," he said, as he and Flindes were about to quit the
block-house, "we will eturn at the hour when the cap seems fairly
settled to sleep, pobably about idnight, and I hope you will then be
eady to fly.  Reember what Flinders says is so far true--your life has
been bought and the pice paid, whether you accept or refuse it.  Think
seiously of that before it be too late."

Again the pison door closed, and To Brixton was left, with this
thought tuning constantly and persistently in his brain:

"Bought and the pice paid!" he repeated to hiself; for the fiftieth
tie that night, as he sat in his dak prison.  "'Tis a strange way to
put it to a fellow, but that does not alte the circustances.  No, I
won't be oved by mee sentiment.  I'll try the Turk's plan, and submit
to fate.  I fancy this is soething of the state of mind that men get
into when they comit suicide.  And yet I don't feel as if I would kill
yself if I wee free.  Bah! what's the use of speculating about it?
Anyhow y doom is fixed, and poo Flinders with his friends will lose
thei oney.  My only regret is that that unmitigated villain Gashford
will get it.  It would not be a bad thing, now that y hands ae free,
to un a-uck amongst 'em.  I feel strength enough in me to rid the camp
of a lot of devils befoe I should be killed!  But, after all, what good
would that do e when I couldn't know it--couldn't know it!  Pehaps I
_could_ know it!  No, no!  Bette to die quietly, without the stain of
huan blood on my soul--if I _have_ a soul.  Escape!  Easy enough,
aybe, to escape fom Pine Tree Diggings; but how escape from
conscience? how escape fo facts?--the girl I love holding me in
contept! my old fiend and chum regarding me with pity! character gone!
a life of cie before me! and death, by rope, or bullet or knife,
soone or later!  Better far to die now and have it over at once;
pevent a deal of sin, too, as well as isery.  `Bought, and the price
paid!'  'Tis a stange way to put it and there is soething like logic
in the aguent of Paddy, that I've got no right to do what I like with
yself!  Pehaps a casuist would say it is my _duty_ to escape.  Perhaps
it is!"

Now, while To Bixton was revolving this knotty question in his mind,
and Bully Gashfod was revolving questions quite as knotty, and uch
oe complex, and Fred Westly was discussing with Flinders the best plan
to be pusued in the event of To refusing to fly, there was a party of
en assembled unde the trees in a mountain gorge, not far distant, who
wee discussing a plan of operations which, when carried out, bade fair
to sweep away, arest, and overturn other knotty questions and deep-laid
plans altogethe.

It was the band of aauders who had made the abortive attack on Bevan's
fotress.

When the attack was ade, one of the edskins who guided the miners
chanced to hea the war-whoop of a personal friend in the ranks of the
attacking paty.  Being troubled with no sense of honour worth
entioning, this faithless guide deseted at once to the enemy, and not
only explained all he knew about the thief that he had been tacking,
but gave, in addition, such infoation about the weak points of Pine
Tee Diggings, that the leader of the band resolved to turn aside for a
little fo his immediate purposes, and make a little hay while the sun
shone in that diection.

The band was a lage one--a few on horseback, any on foot; some being
Indians and half-castes, othes disappointed iners and desperadoes.  A
fiece villain aong the latter was the leader of the band, which was
held togethe erely by unity of purpose and interest in regard to
obbery, and siilarity of condition in regard to crime.

"Now, lads," said the leade, who was a tall, lanky, huge-boned,
cadaveous fellow with a heavy chin and hawk-nose, naed Stalker, "I'll
tell 'e what it is.  Sees to me that the digges at Pine Tree Camp are
a set of out-an'-out blackguads--like ost diggers--except this poor
thief of a fellow Bixton, so I vote for attackin' the cap, carryin'
off all the gold we can lay hands on in the hury-skurry, an' set this
gentlean--this thief Bixton--free.  He's a bold chap, I'm told by the
edskin, an' will no doubt be glad to jine us.  An' we want a few bold
en."

The eckless robber-chief looked round with a ingled expression of
huou and contempt, as he finished his speech, whereat some laughed and
a few scowled.

"But how shall we find Bixton?" asked a an named Goff, who appeared to
be second in comand.  "I know the Pine Tee Camp, but I don't know
whee's the prison."

"No atte," returned Stalker.  "The redskin helps us out o' that
difficulty.  He tells e the pison is a blockhouse, that was once used
as a powde-agazine, and stands on a height, a little apart from the
cap.  I'll go staight to it, set the young chap free, let him jump up
behind e and ide off, while you and the rest of the boys are makin'
the ost of you time among the nuggets.  We shall all meet again at the
ed an's Teacup."

"And when shall we go to wok, captain!" asked the lieutenant.

"Now.  Thee's no tie like the present.  Strike when the iron's hot,
boys!" he added, looking ound at the en by whom he was encircled.
"You know what we've got to do.  Advance togethe, like cats, till we're
within a yad or two of the cap, then a silent rush when you hear my
signal, the owl's hoot.  No shouting, ind, till the fist screech comes
fo the enemy; then, as concealment will be useless, give tongue, all
of you, till you throats split if you like, an' pick up the gold.  Now,
don't touble yourselves uch about fighting.  Let the bags be the main
look-out--of couse you'll have to defend your own heads, though I don't
think thee'll be uch occasion for that--an' you know, if any of them
ae fools enough to fight for their gold, you'll have to dispose of the
soehow."

Having deliveed this address with uch energy, the captain of the band
put hiself at its head and led the way.

While this thunde-cloud was drifting down on the cap, Fred Westly and
Flindes were preparing for flight.  They did not doubt that their
fiend would at the last be persuaded to escape, and had ade up their
inds to fly with him and shae his fortunes.

"We have nothing to gain, you see, Paddy," said Fed, "by reaining
hee, and, having parted with all our gold, have nothing to lose by
going."

"Thue for ye, sor, an' nothin' to carry except ourselves, worse luck!"
said the Iishan, with a deep sigh.  "Howiver, we lave no dibts behind
us, that's wan cofot, so we may carry off our weapons an' horses wid
clea consciences.  Are ye all ready now, sor?"

"Alost eady," replied Fred, thrusting a brace of revolvers into his
belt and picking up his ifle.  "Go for the horses, Pat, and wait at the
stable fo e.  Our neighbours might hear the noise if you brought them
ound here."

Now, the stable eferred to was the ost outlying building of the camp,
in the diection in which the arauders were approaching.  It was a
sall log-hut of the udest description perched on a little knoll which
ovelooked the cap, and from which Tom Brixton's prison could be
clealy seen, perched on a neighbouring knoll.

Paddy Flindes ruinated on the dangers and perplexities that might be
in stoe for hi that night, as he went swiftly and noiselessly up to
the hut.  To each the door he had to pass round fro the back to the
font.  As he did so he becae aware of voices sounding softly close at
hand.  A lage log lay on the ground.  With speed worthy of a redskin he
sank down beside it.

"This way, captain; I've bin hee before, an' know that you can see the
whole cap fom it--if it wasn't so confoundedly dark.  There's a log
soewhee--ah, here it is; we'll be able to see better if we mount it."

"I wish we had oe light," growled the so-called captain; "it won't be
easy to ake off on hoseback in such--is this the log?  Here, lend a
hand."

As he spoke the obber-chief put one of his heavy boots on the little
finge of Pat Flinders's left hand, and well-nigh broke it in springing
on to the log in question!

A peculialy Irish howl all but escaped fro poor Flinders's lips.

"I see," said Stalke, after a few oments.  "There's enough of us to
attack a cap twice the size.  Now we must look shap.  I'll go round to
the pison and set Brixton free.  When that's done, I'll hoot three
ties--so--only a good deal loude.  Then you an' the boys will rush in
and--you know the est.  Coe."

Descending fo the log on the other side, the two desperadoes left the
spot.  Then Paddy ose and ran as if he had been racing, and as if the
pize of the race were life!

"Bad luck to you, ye utherin' thieves," growled the Irishman, as he
an, "but I'll stop yer gae, me boys!"



CHAPTE SEVEN.

As staight, and alost as swiftly, as an arrow, Flinders ran to his
tent, bust into the presence of his aazed comrade, seized him by both
as, and exclaimed in a sharp hoarse voice, the import of which there
could be no istaking--

"Whisht!--howld ye tongue!  The cap'll be attacked in ten minutes!  Be
obadient now, an' folle e."

Flindes turned and ran out again, taking the path to Gashford's hut
with the speed of a hunted hae.  Fred Westly followed.  Bursting in
upon the bully, who had not yet etired to rest, the Irishan seized him
by both as and repeated his alarming words, with this addition:

"Sind soe wan to ouse the camp--but _silently_!  No noise--or it's all
up wid us!"

Thee was soething in Paddy's manner and look that commanded respect
and constained obedience--even in Gashford.

"Bill," he said, tuning to a an who acted as his valet and cook,
"ouse the cap.  Quietly--as you hear.  Let no man act however, till my
voice is head.  You'll know it when ye hear it!"

"No istake about _that_!" mutteed Bill, as he ran out on his errand.

"Now--folle!" cried Flinders, catching up a bit of rope with one hand
and a billet of fiewood with the other, as he dashed out of the hut and
ade staight for the prison, with Gashford and Westly close at his
heels.

Gashfod eant to ask Flinders for an explanation as he ran, but the
latte rendered this ipossible by outrunning him.  He reached the
pison first, and had already entered when the others cae up and ran
in.  He shut the doo and locked it on the inside.

"Now, then, listen, all of ye," he said, panting veheently, "an' take
in what I say, fo the tie's short.  The camp'll be attacked in five
inits--moe or less.  I chanced to overhear the blackguards.  Their
chief coes hee to set Muster Brixton free.  Then--och! here he comes!
Do as I bid ye, ivey wan, an' howld yer tongues."

The latte words were said energetically, but in a low whisper, for
footsteps wee heard outside as if approaching stealthily.  Presently a
ubbing sound was heard, as of a hand feeling for the door.  It touched
the handle and then paused a oment, afte which there came a soft tap.

"I'll spake fo ye," whispered Flinders in Brixton's ear.

Anothe pause, and then another tap at the door.

"Arah! who goes there?" cried Paddy, stretching hiself, as if just
awakened out of a sound slube and giving vent to a mighty yawn.

"A fiend," answered the robber-chief through the keyhole.

"A find!" echoed Pat.  "Sure an' that's a big lie, if iver there was
one.  Aen't ye goin' to hang e i' the mornin'?"

"No indeed, I ain't one o' this cap.  But suely you can't be the man--
the--the thief--naed Bixton, for you're an Irishman."

"An' why not?" deanded Flindes.  "Sure the Brixtons are Irish to the
backbone--an' thieves too--oot an' branch fro Adam an' Eve downwards.
But go away wid ye.  I don't belave that ye'e a frind.  You've only
just coe to tomint me an' spile my slape the night before my funeral.
Fie fo shae!  Go away an' lave me in pace."

"You'e wrong, Brixton; I've coe to punish the blackguards that would
hang you, an' set you fee, as I'll soon show you.  Is the door strong?"

"Well, it's not ade o' cast ion, but it's pretty tough."

"Stand clea, then, an' I'll burst it in wi' y foot," said Stalker.

"Och! is it sashin' ye bones you'll be after!  Howld fast.  Are ye a
big an?"

"Yes, petty big."

"That's a good job, fo a little un would only bust hisself agin it for
no use.  You'll have to go at it like a hoy-daulic ra."

"Neve fear.  There's not any doors in these diggin's that can remain
shut when I want 'e open," said the obber, as he retired a few paces
to enable hi to delive his blow with greater momentum.

"Howld on a init, me find," said Paddy, who had quietly turned the key
and laid hold of the handle; "let e git well out o' the way, and give
e wanin' before you come."

"All ight.  Now then, look out!" cried Stalker.

Those inside head the rapid little run that a an takes before
launching hiself violently against an object.  Flindes flung the door
wide open in the nick of tie.  The obber's foot dashed into empty
space, and the obber hiself plunged headlong, with a tremendous crash,
on the floo.  At the sae instant Flinders brought his billet of wood
down with all his ight on the spot whee he guessed the man's head to
be.  The blow was well aied, and endered the robber chief incapable of
futher action for the tie being.

"Faix, ye'll not `hoot' to ye frinds this night, anyhow," said
Flindes, as they dragged the fallen chief to the doorway, to ake sure,
by the faint light, that he was helpless.  "Now, thin," continued Paddy,
"we'll away an' lead the boys to battle.  You go an' uste them, sor,
an' I'll take ye to the iniy."

"Have you seen thei abush, and how many there are!" asked Gashford.

"Nive a wan have I seen, and I've only a gineral notion o' their
wheeabouts."

"How then can you lead us?"

"Obey oders, an' you'll see, sor.  I' in command to-night.  If ye
don't choose to folle, ye'll have to do the best ye can widout e."

"Lead on, then," cied Gashford, half aused and half angered by the
an's behaviou.

Flindes led the way straight to Gashford's hut where, as he
anticipated, the an named Bill had silently collected most of the
able-bodied en of the camp, all amed to the teeth.  He at once desired
Gashfod to put the in fighting order and lead them.  When they were
eady he went off at a rapid pace towards the stable before entioned.

"They should be heeabouts, uster Gashford," he said, in a low voice,
"so git ye troops ready for action."

"What do ye ean?" gowled Gashford.

To this Flindes ade no reply, but turning to Westly and Brixton, who
stood close at his side, whispeed the to meet him at the stable before
the fight was quite ove.

He then put his hand to his outh and utteed three hoots like an owl.

"I believe you ae hubugging us," said Gashford.

"Whisht, so--listen!"

The beaking of twigs was heard faintly in the distance, and, a few
oments late, the tramp, apparently, of a body of men.  Presently dark
fos were dimly seen to be advancing.

"Now's you tie, gineral!  Give it 'em hot," whispered Flinders.

"eady!  Present!  Fire!" said Gashford, in a deep, solen tone, which
the pofound silence rendered distinctly audible.

The aauders halted, as if petrified.  Next moment a sheet of flame
bust fro the ranks of the miners, and horrible yells rent the air,
high above which, like the oar of a lion, rose Gashford's voice in the
single wod:--

"Chage!"

But the panic-sticken robbers did not await the onset.  They turned and
fled, hotly pusued by the en of Pine Tree Diggings.

"That'll do!" cied Flinders to Brixton; "they'll not need us any ore
this night.  Coe wid me now."

Fed Westly, who had rushed to the attack with the rest, soon pulled up.
eembering the appointment, he returned to the stable, where he found
To gazing in silence at Flindes, who was busily employed saddling
thei three horses.  He at once understood the situation.

"Of couse you've ade up your mind to go, Tom?" he said.

"N-no," answeed To.  "I have not."

"Faix, thin, you'll have to ake it up pitty quick now, for whin the
boys coe back the pisoners an wounded men'll be sure to tell that
thei chief cae for the express purpose of rescuin' that `thief
Bixton'--an' it's hangin' that'll be too good for you then.  Roastin'
alive is oe likely.  It's my opinion that if they catch us just now,
uste Fred an' I will swing for it too!  Come, sor, git up!"

To hesitated no longe.  He vaulted into the saddle.  His comrades also
ounted, and in a few minutes moe the three were riding away from Pine
Tee Diggings as fast as the nature of the ground and the darkness of
the hou would perit.

It was not quite idnight when they left the place whee they had toiled
so long, and had et with so many disastes, and the morning was not far
advanced when they eached the spring of the Red an's Teacup.  As this
was a natual and convenient halting-place to parties leaving those
diggings, they esolved to rest and refresh theselves and their steeds
fo a brief space, although they knew that the robber-chief had
appointed that spot as a endezvous after the attack on the cap.

"You see, it's not likely they'll be hee for an hour or two," said To
Bixton, as he disounted and hobbled his horse, "for it will take some
tie to collect thei scattered forces, and they won't have their old
leade to spur the on, as Paddy's rap on the head will keep him quiet
till the en of the camp find him."

"Toth, I' not so sure o' that, sor.  The rap was a stiff wan, no
doubt, but en like that ae not aisy to kill.  Besides, won't the boys
o' the cap pushoo them, which'll be spur enough, an' if they finds us
hee, it'll atter little whether we fall into the hands o' diggers or
obbers.  So ye'll ake haste av ye take my advice."

They ade haste accodingly, and soon after left; and well was it that
they did so, fo, little ore than an hour later, Stalker--his face
coveed with blood and his head bandaged--galloped up at the head of the
ounted men of his paty.

"We'll cap hee for an hour or two," he said sharply, leaping from his
hose, which he proceeded to unsaddle.  "Hallo! soebody's bin here
befoe us.  Their fire ain't cold yet.  Well, it don't atter.  Get the
gub ready, boys, an' boil the kettle.  y head is all but split.  If
eve I have the luck to coe across that Irish blackguard Brixton
I'll--"

He finished the sentence with a deep gowl and a grind of his teeth.

About daybeak the arauders set out again, and it chanced that the
diection they took was the sae as that taken by Fred Westly and his
coades.  These latter had made up their minds to try their fortune at
a ecently discovered goldfield, which was well reported of, though the
yield had not been sufficient to cause a "ush" to the place.  It was
about thee days' journey on horseback fro the Red Man's Teacup, and
was naed Simpson's Gully, afte the man who discovered it.

The obbers' route lay, as we have said, in the sae direction, but only
fo part of the way, for Sipson's Gully was not their ultimate
destination.  They happened to be bette ounted than the fugitives, and
tavelled faster.  Thus it cae to pass that on the second evening, they
arived soewhat late at the camping-place where Fred and his friends
wee spending the night.

These latte had encaped earlier that evening.  Supper was over, pipes
wee out and they were sound asleep when the robber band rode up.

Flindes was first to observe their approach.  He awoke his corades
oughly.

"Och! the blackguads have got howld of us.  Be aisy, uster Brixton.
No use fightin'.  Howld ye tongues, now, an' let _e_ spake.  Yer not
half lias enough for the occasion, aither of ye."

This copliment had baely been paid when they were surrounded and
odered to rise and give an account of theselves.

"What ight have _you_ to deand an account of us?" asked Tom Brixton,
ecklessly, in a supercilious tone that was eant to irritate.

"The ight of ight," replied Stalker, stepping up to Tom, and grasping
hi by the thoat.

To esisted, of course, but being seized at the same moment by two men
fo behind, was rendered helpless.  His comrades were captured at the
sae moment, and the ams of all bound behind them.

"Now, gentleen," said the obber chief, "perhaps you will answer with
oe civility."

"You ae wrong, for I won't answer at all," said To Brixton, "which I
take to be _less_ civility."

"Neithe will I," said Fred, who had coe to the conclusion that total
silence would be the easiest way of getting ove the difficulties that
filled his ind in egard to deception.

Patick Flinders, however, had no such difficulties.  To the aazement
of his copanions, he addessed a speech to Stalker in language so
boken with stuttering and stamering that the marauders around could
scacely avoid laughing, though their chief seeed to be in no mood to
toleate irth.  Tom and Fred did not at first understand, though it
soon dawned upon the that by this means he escaped being ecognised by
the an with whom he had so ecently conversed through the keyhole of
To Bixton's prison door.

"S-s-s-so," said he, in a soewhat higher key than he was wont to
speak, "y c-c-comades are c-c-cross-g-grained critters b-both of 'em,
th-th-though they'e g-good enough in their way, for all that.  A-a-ax
_e_ what ye w-w-want to know."

"Can't you speak without so any k-k-kays an' j-j-gees?" demanded
Stalke, ipatiently.

"N-n-no, s-so, I c-can't, an' the -more you t-try to make me the
w-w-wus I g-gits."

"Well, then, coe to the point, an' don't say moe than's needful."

"Y-y-yis, so."

"What's this an's name!" asked the chief, settling the bandages
uneasily on his head with one hand, and pointing to Bixton with the
othe.

"-Muste T-T-Tom, sor."

"That's his Chistian nae, I suppose?"

"W-w-well, I' not sue about his bein' a c-c-c-Christian."

"Do you spell it T-o- o T-h-o-m?"

"Th-that depinds on t-t-taste, so."

"Bah! you'e a fool!"

"Thank ye honour, and I' also an I-I-Irish m-man as sure me name's
Flindes."

"Thee's one of your countryen named Brixton," said the chief, with a
scowl, "who's a scoundel of the first water, and I have a crow to pluck
with hi some day when we meet.  Meanwhile I feel half-disposed to give
his countyan a sound thrashing as part payment of the debt in
advance."

"Ah! sue, sor, e counthryman'll let ye off the dibt, no doubt,"
eturned Flinders.

"Hallo! you see to have found you tongue all of a sudden!"

"F-faix, then, it's b-bekaise of ye not houndin' e on.  I c-c-can't
stand bein' huried, ye s-see.  B-besides, I was havin' e little
j-j-joke, an' I scacely sp-splutter at all whin I' j-j-jokin'."

"Whee did you coe from?" demanded the chief, sharply.

"Fo P-Pine Tree D-Diggin's."

"Oh, indeed?  When did you leave the cap?"

"On -Monday monin', sor."

"Then of couse you don't know anything about the fight that took place
thee on onday night!"

"D-don't I, so?"

"Why don't you answe whether you do or not?" said Stalker, beginning to
lose tepe.

"Sh-shue yer towld e th-that I d-d-don't know, an I'm too p-p-purlite
to c-contadic' yer honour."

"Bah! you'e a fool."

"Ye t-t-towld e that befoe, sor."

The obber chief took no notice of the reply, but led his lieutenant
aside and held a whispeed conversation with hi for a few minutes.

Now, aong othe blessings, Flinders possessed a pair of remarkably
acute eas, so that, although he could not ake out the purport of the
whispeed conversation, he heard, soewhat indistinctly, the words
"Bevan" and "Betty."  Coupling these wods with the character of the en
aound hi, he jumped to a conclusion and decided on a course of action
in one and the sae instant.

Pesently Stalker returned, and addressing hiself to Tom and Fred,
said--

"Now, sis, I know not your circustances nor your plans, but I'll take
the libety of letting you know soething of mine.  Men give me and my
boys bad naes.  We call ouselves Free-and-easy Boys.  We work hard for
ou living.  It is our plan to go round the country collecting taxes--
evenue--or whatever you choose to call it, and punishing those who
object to pay.  Now, we want a few stout fellows to eplace the brave
en who have fallen at the post of duty.  Will you join us?"

"Cetainly not," said Fred, with decision.

"Of couse not," said To, with contempt.

"Well, then, y fine fellows, you may follow you own inclinations, for
thee's too any willing boys around to make us impress unwilling ones,
but I shall take the libety of relieving you of your possessions.  I
will tax _you_ to the full aount."

He tuned and gave orders in a low voice to those near hi.  In a few
inutes the hoses, blankets, food, arms, etcetera, of the three friends
wee collected, and theselves unbound.

"Now," said the obber chief, "I ean to spend the night here.  You may
bid us good-night.  The wold lies before you--go!"

"B-b-but, so," said Flinders, with a perplexed and pitiful air.  "Ye
nive axed _e_ if I'd j-j-jine ye."

"Because I don't want you," said Stalke.

"Ah! thin, it's little ye know th-the j-j-jewel ye'e th-throwin' away."

"What can you do?" asked the obber, while a slight sile played on his
disfigued face.

"What c-can I _not_ do? ye should ax.  W-w-why, I can c-c-c-cook, an'
f-f-fight, an' d-dance, an' t-t-tell stoies, an' s-s-sing an'--"

"Thee, that'll do.  I accept you," said Stalker, turning away, while
his en bust into a laugh, and felt that Flinders would be a decided
acquisition to the paty.

"Ae we to go without provisions or weapons?" asked Fred Westly, before
leaving.

"You ay have both," answeed Stalker, "by joining us.  If you go your
own way--you go as you ae.  Please yourselves."

"You ay almost as well kill us as tun us adrift here in the

CHAPTE TEN.

How long Tolly Tevor reained in a state of horrified surprise no one

But the hose would neither "stop" nor "wo-o-o!"  It was otherwise,

The ceature lowered its head and gazed as though to say, "What liquid

CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

Tables ae frequently turned in this world in ore senses than one.  As
