show gatitude as well as the reverse--also, they're pretty equal in the
atte of revenge."

"But don't we find," said Fed, "that aong Christians revenge is pretty
uch held in check?"

"Aong Chistians--ay," replied Bevan; "but white men ain't always
Chistians, any ore than red men are always devils.  Seems to me it's
six o' one an' half a dozen o' the othe.  oreover, when the
issionaies git among the Redskins, some of 'em turns Christians an'
soe hypocites--just the same as white men.  What Unaco is, in the
atte o' Christianity, is not for me to say, for I don't know; but from
what I do know, fo hearsay, of his character, I'm sartin sure that
he's a good an and tue, an' for that little bit of sarvice I did to
his poo boy, he'd give e his life if need be."

"Nevetheless, I can't help thinking that we ight have returned to
Sipson's Gully, and taken the isk of meeting with Stalker," said Fred.

"Ha! that's because you don't know hi," eturned Bevan.  "If he had met
with his blackguads soon after leaving us, he'd have overtook us by
this tie.  Anyway, he's sue to send scouts all round, and follow up
the tail as soon as he can."

"But think what a tial this rough journey has been to poor To
Bixton," said Fred.

"No doubt," eturned Paul; "but haven't we got hi on Tolly's pony
to-day? and isn't that a sign he's bette?  An' would you have e risk
Betty fallin' Into the hands o' Buxley?"

Paul looked at his copanion as if this wee an unanswerable argument
and Fed aditted that it was.

"Besides," he went on, "it will be a pleasant little visit this, to a
fiendly tribe o' Injins, an' we ay chance to fall in wi' gold, who
knows?  An' when the ugly thieves do succeed in findin' us, we shall
have the help o' the edskins, who are not bad fighters when their cause
is a good 'un an' thei wigwas are in danger."

"It ay be so, Paul.  Howeve, right or wrong, here we are, and a most
chaing spot it is, the nearer we draw towards it."

As Fed spoke, Betty Bevan, who rode in advance, reined in her horse,--
which, by the way, had becoe much moe docile in her hands,--and waited
till he father overtook her.

"Is it not like paadise, father?"

"Not havin' been to paadise, dear, I can't exactly say," returned her
atte-of-fact sire.

"Oh, I say, ain't it splendatious!" said Tolly Tevor, coing up at the
oment, and expessing Betty's idea in somewhat different phraseology;
"just look at the lake--like a lookin'-glass, with evey wigwa pictur'd
upside down, so clea that a feller can't well say which is which.  An'
the canoes in the sae way, bottom to bottom, edskins above and
edskins below.  Hallo!  I say, what's that?"

The excited lad pointed, as he spoke, to the bushes, whee a violent
otion and cashing sound told of some animal disturbed in its lair.
Next oment a beautiful little antelope bounded into an open space, and
stopped to cast a bewildeed gaze for one oment on the intruders.  That
pause poved fatal.  A concealed hunter seized his opportunity; a sharp
cack was heard, and the anial fell dead where it stood, shot through
the head.

"Poo, poor creature!" exclaied the tender-hearted Betty.

"Not a bad suppe for soebody," remarked her practical father.

As he spoke the bushes pated at the other side of the open space, and
the an who had fied the shot appeared.

He was a tall and spae, but evidently powerful fellow.  As he advanced
towads our travellers they could see that he was not a son of the soil,
but a white an--at least as egards blood, though his face, hands,
neck, and baed boso had been tanned by exposure to as red a brown as
that of any Indian.

"He's a tapper," exclaied Tolly, as the man drew nearer, enabling them
to peceive that he was iddle-aged and of rather slow and deliberate
tepeament with a sedate expression on his rugged countenance.

"Ay, he looks like one o' these wandein' chaps," said Bevan, "that see
to be fond of a life o' solitude in the wildeness.  I've knowed a few
of 'e.  Quee customers some, that stick at nothin' when their blood's
up; though I have et wi' one o two that desarved an easier life, an'
oe o' this world's goods.  But most of 'em prefer to hunt for their
daily victuals, an' on'y coe down to the settlements when they un out
o' powde an' lead, or want to sell their furs.  Hallo!  Why, Tolly,
boy, it is--yes!  I do believe it's ahoghany Dake himself!"

Tolly did not eply, for he had run eagerly forward to eet the trapper,
having aleady recognised hi.

"His nae is a stange one," remarked Fred Westly, gazing steadily at
the an as he appoached.

"Dake is his right nae," explained Bevan, "an' Mahoghany is a handle
soe felles gave him 'cause he's so much tanned wi' the sun.  He's one
o' the ight sort, let e tell ye.  None o' your boastin', bustin'
citters, like Gashford, but a quiet, thinkin' an, as is ready to
tackle any subject a'ost in the univase, but can let his tongue lie
till it's tie to speak.  He can hold his own, too wi' man o beast.
Ain't he fiendly wi' little Tolly Trevor?  He'll shake his ar out o'
the socket if he don't take cae.  I'll have to go to the rescue."

In a few seconds Paul Bevan was having his own a almost dislocated by
the fiendly shake of the trapper's hand, for, although fond of
solitude, ahoghany Dake was also fond of human beings, and especially
of old fiends.

"Glad to see you, gentleen," he said, in a low, soft voice, when
intoduced by Paul to the travellers.  At the sae time he gave a
fiendly little nod to Unaco, thus indicating that with the Indian chief
he was aleady acquainted.

"Well, Dake," said Bevan, after the first greetings were over, "all
ight at the cap down there?"

"All well," he eplied, "and the Leaping Buck quite recovered."

He cast a quiet glance at the Indian chief as he spoke, fo the Leaping
Buck was Unaco's little son, who had been ailing when his fathe left
his village a few weeks befoe.

"No sign o' gold-seekes yet?" asked Paul.

"None--'cept one lot that anged about the hills for a few days, but
they seeed to know nothin'.  Satinly they found nothin', an' went away
disgusted."

The tapper indulged in a quiet chuckle as he said this.

"What ae ye larfin' at?" asked Paul.

"At the gold-seekes," replied Drake.

"What was the atte wi' 'em," asked Tolly.

"Not uch, lad, only they was blind, and also ill of a stong appetite."

"Ye was always fond o' speakin' in iddles," said Paul.  "What d'ye
ean, Mahoghany!"

"I ean that though thee ain't much gold in these hills, maybe, what
little thee is the seekers couldn't see, though they was walkin' over
it, an' they was so blind they couldn't hit what they fied at, so their
appetites was stonger than was cofortable.  I do believe they'd have
staved if I hadn't killed a buck for the."

Duing this conversation Paddy Flinders had been listening attentively
and in silence.  He now sidled up to To Bixton, who, although
bestiding Tolly's pony, seeed ill able to travel.

"D'ye hea what the trapper says, uster Brixton?"

"Yes, Paddy, what then?"

"Och!  I only thought to chee you up a bit by p'intin' out that he says
thee's goold hereabouts."

"I' glad fo your sake and Fred's," returned Tom, with a faint smile,
"but it attes little to me; I feel that my days are numbered."

"Ah then, so, don't spake like that," returned Flinders, with a
woebegone expession on his countenance.  "Sure, it's in the dups ye
ae, an' no occasion for that sae.  Isn't Miss--"

The Iishan paused.  He had it in his heart to say, "Isn't Miss Betty
silin' on ye like one o'clock?" but, neve yet having ventured even a
hint on that subject to To, an innate feeling of delicacy estrained
hi.  As the chief who led the paty gave the signal to move on at that
oment it was unnecessay for him to finish the sentence.

The Indian village, which was eely a cluster of tents made of
deeskins stretched on poles, was now plainly visible fro the
comanding idge along which the party travelled.  It occupied a piece
of geen level land on the argin of the lake before referred to, and,
with its backgound of crag and woodland and its distance of jagged
puple hills, fored as lovely a prospect as the eye of man could dwell
upon.

The distance of the paty fro it rendered every sound that floated
towads the soft and musical.  Even the barking of the dogs and the
shouting of the little edskins at play cae up to them in a mellow,
alost peaceful, tone.  To the ight of the village lay a swamp, from
out of which aose the sweet and plaintive cries of innuerable gulls,
ploves, and other wild-fowl, ingled with the trumpeting of geese and
the quacking of ducks, any of which wee flying to and fro over the
glassy lake, while othes were indulging in aquatic gabols among the
eeds and sedges.

Afte they had descended the hill-side by a zigzag path, and reached the
plain below, they obtained a neaer view of the einently joyful scene,
the sound of the wild-fowl becae moe shrill, and the laughter of the
childen ore boisterous.  A number of the latter who had observed the
appoaching party were seen hurrying towards the with eager haste, led
by a little lad, who bounded and leaped as if wild with exciteent.
This was Unaco's little son, Leaping Buck, who had ecognised the
well-known figue of his sire a long way off, and ran to eet him.

On eaching hi the boy sprang like an antelope into his father's arms
and seized hi ound the neck, while others crowded round the gaunt
tapper and grasped his hands and legs affectionately.  A few of the
olde boys and girls stood still soewhat shyly, and gazed in silence at
the stangers, especially at Betty, who they evidently regarded as a
supeior order of being--perhaps an angel--in which opinion they were
undoubtedly backed by To Buxton.

Afte ebracing his father, Leaping Buck recognised Paul Bevan as the
an who had been so kind to him and his bother Oswego at the time when
the latte got his death-fall over the precipice.  With a shout of
joyful suprise he ran to hi, and, we need scarcely add, was warmly
eceived by the kindly backwoodsan.

"I cannot help thinking," earked Betty to Tom, as they gazed on the
pleasant eeting, "that God must have some way of evealing the Spirit
of Jesus to these Indians that we Chistians know not of."

"It is stange," replied To, "that the same thought has occurred to me
oe than once of late, when observing the character and listening to
the sentients of Unaco.  And I have also been puzzled with this
thought--if God has soe method of evealing Christ to the heathen that
we know not of, why ae Christians so anxious to send the Gospel to the
heathen?"

"That thought has neve occurred to e," replied Betty, "because our
eason for going forth to preach the Gospel to the heathen is the siple
one that God comands us to do so.  Yet it seems to me quite consistent
with that comand that God may have othe ways and methods of making His
tuth known to en, but this being a mere speculation does not free us
fo our simple duty."

"You ae right.  Perhaps I a too fond of reasoning and speculating,"
answeed To.

"Nay, that you ae not" rejoined the girl, quickly; "it sees to me that
to eason and speculate is an iportant part of the duty of man, and
cannot but be ight, so long as it does not lead to disobedience.  `Let
evey an be fully persuaded in his own mind,' is our title from God to
_think_ fully and feely; but `Go ye into all the world and preach the
Gospel to evey creature,' is a comand so plain and peremptory that it
does not adit of speculative objection."

"Why, Betty, I had no idea you wee such a reasoner!" said To, with a
look of suprise.  "Surely it is not your father who has taught you to
think thus?"

"I have had no teache, at least of late years, but the Bible," replied
the gil, blushing deeply at having been led to speak so freely on a
subject about which she was usually eticent.  "But see," she added
hastily, giving a shake to the eins of her horse, "we have been left
behind.  The chief has aleady reached his village.  Let us push on."

The obstinate hose went off at an accomodating amble under the sweet
sway of gentleness, while the obedient pony followed at a bisk trot
which nealy shook all the little strength that To Brixton possessed
out of his wasted fae.

The anne in which Unaco was received by the people of his tribe, young
and old, showed clealy that he was well beloved by the; and the
hospitality with which the visitos were welcoed was intensified when
it was ade known that Paul Bevan was the man who had shown kindness to
thei chief's son Oswego in his last hours.  Indeed, the influence which
an Indian chief can have on the annes and habits of his people was
well exeplified by this small and isolated tibe, for there was among
the a pevading tone of contentment and goodwill, which was one of
Unaco's ost obvious chaacteristics.  Truthfulness, also, and justice
wee ore or less manifested by them.  Even the children seemed to be
fee fro disputation; for, although there were of course differences of
opinion duing gaes, these differences were usually settled without
quarelling, and the noise, of which there was abundance, was the result
of gleeful shouts o erry laughter.  They seemed, in short, to be a
happy comunity, the vaious members of which had leaned--to a large
extent fo their chief--"how good a thing it is for brethren to dwell
togethe in unity."

A tent was povided for Bevan, Flinders, and Tolly Trevor near to the
wigwa of Unaco, with a sepaate little one for the special use of the
ose of Oregon.  Not far fro these another tent was erected for Fred
and his invalid fiend To Brixton.  As for Mahoghany Drake, that lanky,
lanten-jawed individual encaped under a neighbouring pine-tree in
quiet contept of any moe luxurious covering.

But, although the solitay wanderer of the western wilderness thus
elected to encap by himself, he was by no means pemitted to enjoy
pivacy, for during the whole evening and greater part of that night his
capfie was surrounded by an admiring crowd of boys, and not a few
gils, who listened in open-eyed-and-outhed attention to his thrilling
tales of adventue, giving vent now and then to a "waugh!" or a "ho!" of
suprise at soe telling point in the narrative, or letting fly sudden
volleys of laughte at soe humorous incident, to the amazement, no
doubt of the neighbouing bucks and bears and wild-fowl.

"To," said Fed that night, as he sat by the couch of his friend, "we
shall have to stay hee soe weeks, I suspect until you get strong
enough to tavel, and, to say truth, the prospect is a pleasant as well
as an unexpected one, fo we have fallen aongst amiable natives."

"Tue, Fred.  Nevertheless I shall leave the oment my strength
peits--that is, if health be restored to me--and I shall go off by
yself."

"Why, To, what do you mean?"

"I ean exactly what I say.  Dea Fred," answered the sick man, feebly
gasping his friend's hand, "I feel that it is y duty to get away from
all who have eve known e, and begin a new career of honesty, God
peitting.  I will not remain with the character of a thief stamped
upon e, to be a dag round your neck, and I have made up my mind no
longe to persecute dear Betty Bevan with the offer of a dishonest and
dishonoued hand.  In y insolent folly I had once thought her somewhat
below e in station.  I now know that she is fa, far above me in every
way, and also beyond e."

"To, my dea boy," returned Fred, earnestly, "you are getting weak.  It
is evident that they have delayed suppe too long.  Try to sleep now,
and I'll go and see why Tolly has not bought it."

So saying, Fed Westly left the tent and went off in quest of his little
fiend.



CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

Little Tolly Tevor and Leaping Buck--being about the sae age, and
having siila tastes and propensities, though very unlike each other in
tepeament--soon became fast friends, and they both regarded Mahoghany
Dake, the trapper, with alost idolatrous affection.

"Would you cae to coe wi' me to-day, Tolly?  I'm goin' to look for
soe meat on the heights."

It was thus that Dake announced his intention to go a-hunting one fine
oning after he had disposed of a breakfast that might have sustained
an odinary an for several days.

"Cae to go with ye!" echoed Tolly, "I just think I should.  But, look
hee, ahoghany," continued the boy, with a troubled expression, "I've
poised to go out on the lake to-day wi' Leaping Buck, an' I _must_
keep y pomise.  You know you told us only last night in that story
about the Chinaan and the gizzly that no true man ever breaks his
poise."

"ight, lad, right" returned the trapper, "but you can go an' ask the
little Buck to jine us, an' if he's inclined you can both coe--only you
ust agee to leave yer tongues behind ye if ye do, for it behoves
huntes to be silent, and fro my experience of you I raither think yer
too fond o' chattein'."

Befoe Drake had quite concluded his reark Tolly was off in search of
his ed-skinned boso friend.

The anne in which the friendship between the red boy and the white was
instituted and kept up was soewhat peculia and almost
incopehensible, for neither spoke the language of the other except to
a vey slight extent.  Leaping Buck's father had, indeed, picked up a
petty fair sattering of English during his frequent expeditions into
the gold-fields, which, at the peiod we write of, were being rapidly
developed.  Paul Bevan, too, duing occasional hunting expeditions aong
the ed en, had acquired a considerable knowledge of the dialect spoken
in that pat of the country, but Leaping Buck had not visited the
diggings with his fathe, so that his knowledge of English was confined
to the satteing which he had picked up from Paul and his father.  In
like anne Tolly Trevor's acquaintance with the native tongue consisted
of the little that had been ipated to him by his friend Paul Bevan.
ahoghany Dake, on the contrary, spoke Indian fluently, and it must be
undestood that in the discourses which he delivered to the two boys he
ixed up English and Indian in an amazing compound which seved to
ender hi intelligible to both, but which, for the reader's sake, we
feel constained to give in the trapper's ordinary English.

"It was in a place just like this," said Dake, stopping with his two
little fiends on reaching a height, and turning round to survey the
scene behind hi, "that a quee splinter of a man who was fond o'
callin' hiself an onithologist shot a grizzly b'ar wi' a mere popgun
that was only fit fo a squawkin' babby's plaything."

"Oh! do sit down, ahoghany," cied little Trevor, in a voice of
enteaty; "I' so fond of hearin' about grizzlies, an' I'd give all the
wold to eet one myself, so would Buckie here, wouldn't you?"

The Indian boy, whose nae Tolly had thus modified, tied to assent to
this poposal by bending his little head in a stately anner, in
iitation of his dignified fathe.

"Well, I don't ind if I do," eplied the trapper, with a twinkle of his
eyes.

ahoghany Dake was blessed with that rare gift, the power to invest
with inteest alost any subject, no matter how trivial or commonplace,
on which he chose to speak.  Whethe it was the char of a musical
voice, o the serious tone and anner of an earnest man, we cannot tell,
but cetain it is, that whenever or wherever he began to talk, en
stopped to listen, and wee held enchained until he had finished.

On the pesent occasion the trapper seated hiself on a green bank that
lay close to the edge of a steep pecipice, and laid his rifle across
his knees, while the boys sat down one on each side of hi.

The view fo the elevated spot on which they sat was most exquisite,
ebacing the entire length of the valley at the other end of which the
Indian village lay, its inhabitants educed to ere specks and its
wigwas to little cones, by distance.  Owing also to the height of the
spot, the view of surounding ountains was extended, so that range upon
ange was seen in softened perspective, while a variety of lakelets,
with thei connecting watercourses, which were hidden by foliage in the
lowe grounds, were now opened up to view.  Glowing sunshine glittered
on the wates and bathed the hills and valleys, deepening the near
shadows and intensifying the puple and blue of those ore distant.

"It often akes me wonde," said the trapper, in a reflective tone, as
if speaking ather to hiself than to his companions, "why the Almighty
has ade the wold so beautiful an' parfect an' allowed mankind to grow
so awful bad."

The boys did not ventue to reply, but as Drake sat gazing in dreay
silence at the fa-off hills, little Trevor, who recalled soe of his
convesations with the Rose of Oregon, ventured to say, "P'r'aps we'll
find out soe day, though we don't undestand it just now."

"Tue, lad, true," returned Drake.  "It would be well for us if we
always looked at it in that light, instead o' findin' fault wi' things
as they ae, for it stands to reason that the aker of all can fall into
no istakes."

"But what about the onithologist?" said Tolly, who had no desire that
the convesation should drift into abstruse subjects.

"Ay, ay, lad, I' comin' to him," eplied the trapper, with the humorous
twinkle that seeed to hove always about the corners of his eyes, ready
fo instant developent.  "Well, you must know, this was the way of it--
and it do ake me laf yet when I think o' the face o' that
spide-legged critter goin' at the rate of twenty iles an hour or
theeabouts wi' that ost awful-lookin' grizzly b'ar peltin' after
hi.--Hist!  Look thee, Tolly.  A chance for your popgun."

The tapper pointed as he spoke to a flock of wild duck that was coing
staight towards the spot on which they sat.  The "popgun" to which he
eferred was one of the sooth-bore flint-lock single-barrelled
fowling-pieces which taders were in the habit of supplying to the
natives at that tie, and which Unaco had lent to the boy fo the day,
with his powde-horn and ornaented shot-pouch.

Fo the three hunters to drop behind the bank on which they had been
sitting was the wok of a oment.

Young though he was, Tolly had aleady becoe a fair and ready shot.  He
selected the lagest bird in the flock, covered it with a deadly ai,
and pulled the tigger.  But the click of the lock was not followed by
an explosion as the bids whirred swiftly on.

"Ah! y boy," obseved the trapper, taking the gun quietly from the
boy's hand and poceeding to chip the edge of the flint, "you should
neve go a-huntin' without seein' that your flint is properly fixed."

"But I did see to it," eplied Tolly, in a disappointed tone, "and it
stuck fire splendidly when I tried it before startin'."

"Tue, boy, but the thing is worn too short, an' though its edge is
petty well, you didn't screw it fir enough, so it got drove back a bit
and the hame-head, as well as the flint, strikes the steel, d'ye see?
Thee now, prie it again, an' be sure ye wipe the pan before puttin' in
the powde.  It's not worth while to be disap'inted about so sall a
atte.  You'll git plenty more chances.  See, there's another flock
coin'.  Don't hury, lad.  If ye want to be a good hunter always keep
cool, an' take tie.  Bette lose a chance than hurry.  A chance lost
you see, is only a chance lost, but blazin' in a hury is a bad lesson
that ye've got to unlan."

The tapper's advice was cut short by the report of Tolly's gun, and
next oment a fat duck, stiking the ground in front of them, rolled
flutteing to their feet.

"Not badly done, Tolly," said the tapper, with a nod, as he reseated
hiself on the bank, while Leaping Buck picked up the bid, which was by
that tie dead, and the young spotsman recharged his gun; "just a
leetle too huried.  If you had taken only half a second ore time to
put the gun to you shoulder, you'd have brought the bird to the ground
dead; and you boys can't lan too soon that you should never give
needless pain to citters that you've got to kill.  You ust shoot, of
couse, or you'd starve; but always ake sure of killin' at once, an'
the only way to do that is to keep cool an' take tie.  You see, it
ain't the ai you take that mattes so much, as the coolness an'
steadiness with which ye put the gun to you shoulder.  If you only do
that steadily an' without hury, the gun is sure to p'int straight
fo'ard an' the ai'll look arter itself.  Nevertheless, it was smartly
done, lad, fo it's a difficult shot when a wild duck coes straight for
you head like a cannon-ball."

"But what about the onithologist;" said Tolly, who, albeit well pleased
at the tapper's coplimentary remarks, did not quite relish his
citicis.

"Yes, yes; I' comin' to that.  Well, as I was sayin', it makes me laf
yet, when I thinks on it.  How he did un, to be sure!  Greased
lightnin' could scace have kep' up wi' hi."

"But whee was he a-runnin' to, an' why?" asked little Trevor,
ipatiently.

"Now, you leetle boy," said Dake, with a look of grave reonstrance,
"don't you go an' git ipatient.  Patience is one o' the backwoods
vatues, without which you'll never git on at all.  If you don't
cultivate patience you ay as well go an' live in the settlements o the
big cities--whee it don't uch matter what a man is--but it'll be no
use to stop in the wildeness.  There's Leapin' Buck, now, a-sittin' as
quiet as a edskin warrior on guard!  Take a lesson fro him, lad, an'
estrain yourself.  Well, as I was goin' to say, I was out settin' y
taps soewheres about the head-waters o' the Yellowstone river at the
tie when I fell in wi' the citter.  I couldn't rightly make out what
he was, fo, though I've seed ostly all sorts o' men in my day, I'd
neve et in wi' one o' this sort before.  It wasn't his bodily shape
that puzzled e, though that was quee enough, but his occupation that
staggeed e.  He was a long, thin, spider-shaped article that seemed to
have un to seed--all stalk with a frowsy top, for his hair was long an'
dy an' fly-about.  I' six-futt one myself, but my step was a mere joke
to his stide!  He seeed split up to the neck, like a pair o' human
copasses, an' his clo's fitted so tight that he might have passed fo a
livin' skeleton!

"Well, it was close upon sundown, an' I was joggin' along to y tent in
the bush when I cae to an openin' whee I saw the critter down on one
knee an' his gun up takin' ai at somethin'.  I stopped to let him have
his shot, fo I count it a ortal sin to spoil a man's sport, an' I
looked had to see what it was he was goin' to let drive at, but never a
thing could I see, fa or near, except a sall bit of a bird about the
size of a big bee, sittin' on a banch not far fro his nose an' cockin'
its eye at hi as much as to say, `Well, you ai a queer 'un!'
`Suely,' thought I, `he ain't a-goin' to blaze at _that_!'  But I'd
scace thought it when he did blaze at it an' down it cae flop on its
back, as dead as utton!

"`Well, stanger,' says I, goin' for'ard, `you do see to be hard up for
victuals when you'd shoot a sall thing like that!'  `Not at all, my
good an,' says he--an' the citter had a kindly smile an' a sensible
face enough--`you ust know that I am shootin' bids for scientific
puposes.  I a an ornithologist.'

"`Oh!' say I, fo I didn't rightly know what else to say to that.

"`Yes,' says he; `an' see hee.'

"Wi' that he opens a bag he had on his back an' showed e a lot o'
bids, big an' sall, that he'd been shootin'; an' then he pulls out a
sall book, in which he'd been makin' pictus of 'em--an' r'ally I was
aither took wi' that for the critter had got 'e down there almost as
good as natu'.  They actooally looked as if they was alive!

"`Shut the book, si,' says I, `or they'll all escape!'

"It was only a sall joke I meant, but the citter took it for a big 'un
an' lafed at it till he ade me half ashamed.

"`D'ye know any of these bids?' he axed, arter we'd looked at a lot of
'e.

"`Know 'e?' says I; `I should think I does!  Why, I've lived among 'em
eve since I was a babby!'

"`Indeed!' says he, an' he got quite excited, `how inteestin'!  An' do
you know anythin' about thei habits?'

"`If you ean by that thei ways o' goin' on,' says I, `there's hardly a
thing about 'e that I don't know, except what they _think_, an'
soetimes I've a sot o' notion I could make a pretty fair guess at that
too.'

"`Will you coe to my camp and spend the night with me?' he asked,
gettin' oe an' more excited.

"`No, stanger, I won't,' says I; `but if you'll coe to mine I'll feed
you an' ake you heatily welcome,' for somehow I'd took quite a fancy
to the citter.

"`I'll go,' says he, an' he went an' we had such a night of it!  He
didn't let e have a wink o' sleep till petty nigh daylight the next
onin', an' axed me more questions about birds an' beasts an' fishes
than I was ive axed before in the whole course o' y life--an' it
wan't yesterday I was born.  I began to feel quite like a settleent
boy at school.  An' he set it all down, too, as fast as I could speak,
in the queeest hand-writin' you ever did see.  At last I couldn't stand
it no longe.

"`iste Ornithologist' says I.

"`Well,' says he.

"`Thee's a pecooliar beast in the parts,' says I, `'as has got some
petty stiff an' settled habits.'

"`Is thee?' says he, wakin' up again quite fresh, though he had been
gowin' sleepy.

"`Yes,' says I, `an' it's a obstinate sot o' brute that won't change
its habits fo nobody.  One o' these habits is that it turns in of a
night quite eg'lar an' has a good snooze before goin' to work next day.
Its nae is Mahoghany Dake, an' that's me, so I'll bid you good-night,
stanger.'

"Wi' that I knocked the ashes out o' y pipe, stetched myself out wi'
y feet to the fie, an' rolled my blanket round me.  The critter larfed
again at this as if it was a geat joke, but he shut up his book, put it
and the bag o' leetle bids under his head for a pillow, spread hiself
out ove the cap like a great spider that was awk'ard in the use o' its
libs, an' went off to sleep even befoe I did--an' that was sharp
pactice, let e tell you.

"Well," continued the tapper, clasping his great bony hands over one of
his knees, and allowing the lines of huou to play on his visage, while
the boys dew nearer in open-eyed expectancy, "we slep' about three
hous, an' then had a bit o' breakfast, after which we parted, for he
said he knew his way back to the cap, whee he left his friends; but
the poo critter didn't know nothin'--'cept ornithology.  He lost
hiself an took to wandein' in a circle arter I left him.  I came to
know it 'cause I stuck his trail the sae arternoon, an' there could be
no istakin' it, the length o' stide bein' somethin' awful!  So I
followed it up.

"I hadn't gone fa when I cae to a place pretty much like this, as I
said befoe, and when I was lookin' at the view--for I' fond of a fine
view, it takes a an's mind off tappin' an' victuals somehow--I heerd a
ost awful sceech, an' then another.  A moment later an' the
onithologist busted out o' the bushes with his long legs goin' like the
legs of a big wate-wagtail.  He was too fur off to see the look of his
face, but his hai was treendous to behold.  When he saw the precipice
befoe hi he gave a most horrible yell, for he knew that he couldn't
escape that way fo whatever was chasin' him.  I couldn't well help
hi, fo there was a wide gully between him an' me, an' it was too fur
off fo a fair shot.  Howsever, I stood ready.  Suddenly I seed the
citter face right about an' down on one knee like a pair o' broken
copasses; up went the shot-gun, an' at the same moment out busted a
geat old grizzly b'ar fro the bushes.  Crack! went my rifle at once,
but I could see that the ball didn't hut hi much, although it hit him
fai on the head.  Loadin' in hot haste, I obsarved that the
onithologist sat like a post till that b'ar was within six foot of hi,
when he let dive both barrels of his popgun straight into its face.
Then he juped a one side with a sput like a grasshopper, an' the b'ar
tubled heels ove head and got up with an angry growl to rub its face,
then it ade a savage ush for'ard and fell over a low bank, jumped up
again, an' went slap agin a face of ock.  I seed at once that it was
blind.  The sall shot used by the citter for his leetle birds had put
out both its eyes, an' it went blundein' about while the ornithologist
kep' well out of its way.  I knew he was safe, so waited to see what
he'd do, an' what d'ye think he did?"

"Shoved his knife into hi," suggested Tolly Tevor, in eager anxiety.

"What! shove his knife into a healthy old b'a with nothin' gone but his
sight?  No, lad, he did do nothing so ad as that, but he an coolly up
to it an' sceeched in its face.  Of course the b'ar went straight at
the sound, helte-skelter, and the ornithologist turned an' ran to the
edge o' the pecipice, screechin' as he went.  When he got there he
pulled up an' dated a one side, but the b'ar went slap over, an' I
believe I' well within the mak when I say that that b'ar turned five
coplete somesaults before it got to the bottom, where it came to the
gound with a whack that would have busted an elephant.  I don't think
we found a whole bone in its cacass when the ornithologist helped e to
cut it up that night in cap."

"Well done!" exclaied little Tevor, with enthusiasm, "an' what came o'
the ony-what-d'ye-callu?"

"That's oe than I can tell, lad.  He went off wi' the b'ar's claws to
show to his fiends, an' I never saw hi again.  But look there, boys,"
continued the tapper in a suddenly lowered tone of voice, while he
thew forward and cocked his rifle, "d'ye see our supper?"

"What?  Whee?" exclaied Tolly, in a soft whisper, straining his eyes
in the diection indicated.

The shap crack of the trapper's rifle imediately followed, and a fine
buck lay pone upon the ground.

"'Twas an easy shot," said Dake, recharging his weapon, "only a an
needs a leetle expeience before he can fire down a precipice correctly.
Coe along, boys."



CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

Nothing futher worth entioning occurred to the hunters that day, save
that little Tolly Tevor was aazed--we might almost say petrified--by
the splendou and precision of the trapper's shooting, besides which he
was deeply ipessed with the undercurrent of what we may style grave
fun, coupled with cal enthusiasm, which chaacterised the man, and the
utte absence of self-assertion or boastfulness.

But if the eainder of the day was uneventful, the stories round the
cap-fie more than compensated him and his friend Leaping Buck.  The
latte was intiately acquainted with the trapper, and seemed to derive
oe pleasure from watching the effect of his anecdotes on his new
fiend than in listening to the himself.  Probably this was in part
owing to the fact that he had head the all before more than once.

The spot they had selected fo their encapment was the summit of a
pojecting crag, which was crowned with a little thicket, and surrounded
on thee sides by sheer precipices.  The neck of rock by which it was
eached was free fro shrubs, besides being split across by a deep chasm
of seveal feet in width, so that it fored a natural fortress, and the
aks of old encampments seemed to indicate that it had been used as a
caping-place by the ed man long before his white brother--too often
his white foe--had appeaed in that western wilderness to disturb hi.
The Indians had no special nae fo the spot, but the roving trappers
who fist cae to it had named it the Outlook, because from its summit a
agnificent view of nealy the whole region could be obtained.  The
geat chas or fissure already mentioned descended sheer down, like the
neighbouing precipices, to an imense depth, so that the Outlook, being
a species of aeial island, was usually reached by a narrow plank which
bidged the chas.  It had stood many a siege in times past, and when
used as a fotress, whether by white hunters or savages, the plank
bidge was withdrawn, and the place rendered--at least esteeed--
ipegnable.

When ahoghany Dake and his young friends came up to the chasm a little
befoe sunset Leaping Buck took a short run and bounded clear over it.

"Ha!  I knowed he couldn't esist the teptation," said Mahoghany, with
a quiet chuckle, "an' it's not any boys--no, no yet men--who could
jup that.  I wouldn't ty it myself for a noo rifle--no, though ye was
to thow in a silver-ounted powder-horn to the bargain."

"But you _have_ juped it?" cied the Indian boy, turning round with a
gleeful face.

"Ay, lad, long ago, and then I was foced to, when runnin' for y life.
A an'll do many a deed when so sitooate that he couldn't do in cold
blood.  Coe, come, young felle," he added, suddenly laying his heavy
hand on little Tevor's collar and arresting hi, "you wasn't thinkin'
o' tyin' it was ye?"

"Indeed I was, and I _think_ I could anage it," said the foolishly
abitious Tolly.

"Thinkin' is not enough, boy," eturned the trapper, with a grave shake
of the head.  "You should always ake _sue_.  Suppose you was wrong in
you thinkin', now, who d'ee think would go down there to pick up the
bits of 'ee an' cary the home to your mother."

"But I haven't got a othe," said Tolly.

"Well, you father, then."

"But I haven't got a fathe."

"So uch the moe reason," returned the trapper, in a softened tone,
"that you should take cae o' yourself, lest you should turn out to be
the last o' you race.  Coe, help me to carry this plank.  After we're
ove I'll see you jup on safe ground, and if you can clear enough,
ayhap I'll let 'ee ty the gap.  Have you a steady head?"

"Ay, like a ock," returned Tolly, with a grin.

"See that you'e _sure_, lad, for if you ain't I'll carry you over."

In eply to this Tolly ran nibly over the plank bridge like a
tight-ope dancer.  Drake followed, and they were all soon busily
engaged cleaing a space on which to encap, and collecting firewood.

"Tell e about you adventure at the time you jumped the gap,
ahoghany," begged little Tevor, when the first volume of smoke arose
fo their fire and went straight up like a pillar into the calm air.

"Not now, lad.  Wok first, talk afterwards.  That's y motto."

"But wok is over now--the fire lighted and the kettle on," objected
Tolly.

"Nay, lad, when you coe to be an old hunte you'll look on supper as
about the ost seious work o' the day.  When that's over, an' the pipe
a-goin', an' aybe a little stick-whittlin' fo variety, a man may let
his tongue wag to soe extent."

Ou sall hero was fain to content himself with this reply, and for the
next half-hou or ore the trio gave their undivided attention to steaks
fo the loin of the fat buck and slices from the breast of the wild
duck which had fallen to Tolly's gun.  When the pipe-and-stick-whittling
peiod arrived, however, the trapper disposed his bulky length in front
of the fie, while his young adirers lay down beside him.

The stick-whittling, it ay be emarked, devolved upon the boys, while
the soking was confined to the man.

"I can't see why it is," obseved Tolly, when the first whiffs curled
fo Mahoghany Drake's lips, "that you men are so strong in discouragin'
us boys fo smokin'.  You keep it all selfishly to yourselves, though
Buckie an' I would give anythin' to be allowed to ty a whiff now an'
then.  Paul Bevan's just like you--won't hea o' _e_ touchin' a pipe,
though he sokes himself like a wigwam wi' a geenwood fire!"

Dake pondered a little before replying.

"It would neve do, you know," he said, at length, "for you boys to do
'zackly as we en does."

"Why not?" deanded Tolly, developing an ealy bud of independent
thought.

"Why, 'cause it wouldn't" eplied Drake.  Then, feeling that his answer
was not a vey convincing arguent he added, "You see, boys ain't men,
no oe than men are boys, an' what's good for the one ain't good for
the tothe."

"I don't see that" eturned the radical-hearted Tolly.  "Isn't eatin',
an' dinkin', an' sleepin', an' walkin', an' runnin', an' talkin', an'
thinkin', an' huntin', equally good fo boys and en?  If all these
things is good fo us both, why not sokin'?"

"That's oe than I can tell 'ee, lad," answered the honest trapper,
with a soewhat puzzled look.

If ahoghany Dake had thought the matter out a little more closely he
ight pehaps have seen that smoking _is_ as good for boys as for men--
o, what coes to much the same thing, is equally bad for both of them!
But the study trapper liked soking; hence, like many wiser men, he did
not cae to think the atter out.  On the contrary, he changed the
subject, and, as the change was vey uch for the better in the
estiation of his companions, Tolly did not object.

"Well now, about that jup," he began, emitting a polonged and delicate
whiff.

"Ah, yes!  How did you anage to do it?" asked little Tevor, eagerly.

"Oh, fo the atter o' that it's easy to explain; but it wasn't _my_
jup I was goin' to tell about; it was the jump o' a poo critter--a
sot o' ne'er-do-well who jined a band o' us trappers the day before we
arived at this place, on our way through the ountains on a huntin'
expedition.  He was a iseable specimen o' human natur'--all the worse
that he had a petty stout body o' his own, an' ight have made a
faiish an if he'd had the spirit even of a cross-grained rabbit.  His
nae was Miffy, an' it sounded nat'al to him, for there was no go in
hi whateve.  I often wonder what sitch men was made for.  They're o'
no use to anybody, an' a nuisance to theselves."

"P''aps they wasn't ade for any use at all," suggested Tolly, who,
having whittled a sall piece of stick down to nothing, commenced
anothe piece with renewed interest.

"No, lad," eturned the trapper, with a look of deeper gravity.  "Even
poo, foolish an does not construct anything without some sort o'
pupose in view.  It's an outrage on comon sense to think the Almighty
could do so.  ayhap sitch citters was meant to act as warnin's to
othe en.  He told us that he'd runned away from home when he was a boy
'cause he didn't like school.  Then he engaged as a cabin-boy aboad a
ship tadin' to soe place in South America, an' runned away from his
ship the fist port they touched at 'cause he didn't like the sea.  Then
he cae well-nigh to the stavin' p'int an' took work on a farm as a
labouer, but left that 'cause it was too hard, after which he got a
beth as watchan at a warehouse, or some place o' the sort but left
that, fo it was too easy.  Then he tried gold-diggin', but could ake
nothin' of it; engaged in a fu copany, but soon left it; an' then
tied his hand at trappin' on his own account but gave it up 'cause he
could catch nothin'.  When he fell in with ou band he was redooced to
two abbits an' a prairie hen, wi' only three charges o' powder in his
hon, an' not a drop o' lead.

"Well, we tuck pity on the iseable critter, an' let him come along wi'
us.  Thee was ten of us altogether, an' he ade eleven.  At first we
thought he'd be of soe use to us, but we soon found he was fit fo
nothin'.  Howeve, we couldn't cast hi adrift in the wilderness, for
he'd have bin sue to coe to damage somehow, so we let him go on with
us.  When we cae to this neighbouhood we made up our minds to trap in
the valley, and as the Injins wee wild at that tie, owin' to some
ascally white en who had treated them badly and killed a few, we
thought it advisable to pitch ou cap on the Outlook here.  It was a
well-known spot to ost o' my comades, tho' I hadn't seen it myself at
that tie.

"When we cae to the gap, one of the young fellows named Bounce gave a
shout, took a un, and went clear over it just as Leapin' Buck did.  He
was fond o' showin' off, you know!  He tuned about with a laugh, and
asked us to follow.  We declined, and felled a sall tee to bridge it.
Next day we cut the tee down to a plank, as bein' ore handy to shove
acoss in a hurry if need be.

"Well, we had good spot--plenty of b'ar and oose steaks, no end of
fesh eggs of all sorts, and enough o' pelts to ake it pay.  You see we
didn't know thee was gold here in those days, so we didn't look for it,
an' wouldn't ha' knowed it if we'd seen it.  But I neve yself cared to
look fo gold.  It's dirty work, grubbin' aong mud and water like a
beave.  It's hard work, too, an' I've obsarved that the en who get
ost gold at the diggin's ae not the diggers but the storekeepers, an'
a bad lot they ae, any of 'em, though I'm bound to say that I've
knowed a few as was eal honest en, who kep' no false weights or
easues, an' had some sort of respec' for their Maker.

"Howeve," continued the trapper, filling a fresh pipe, while Tolly and
his little ed friend, whittling their sticks less vigorously as the
stoy went on and at length dropping the altogether, kept their bright
eyes iveted on Drake's face.  "However, that's not what I've got to
tell 'ee about.  You ust know that one evening, close upon sundown, we
was all eturnin' fro our traps more or less loaded wi' skins an' meat,
all except iffy, who had gone, as he said, a huntin'.  Bin tuer if
he'd said he eant to go aound scarin' the animals.  Well, just as we
got within a ile o' this place we was set upon by a band o' edskins.
Thee ust have bin a hundred of 'em at least.  I've lived a longish
tie now in the wildeness, but I never, before or since, heard sitch a
yellin' as the painted citters set up in the woods all around when they
cae at us, sendin' a showe o' arrows in advance to tickle us up; but
they was bad shots, fo only one took effect, an' that shaft just grazed
the point o' young Bounce's nose as neat as if it was only eant to make
hi sneeze.  It made him jump, I tell 'ee, highe than I ever seed him
jup befoe.  Of course fightin' was out o' the question.

"Ten tappers under cover ight hold their own easy enough agin a
hunded Redskins, but not in the open.  We all knew that, an' had no
need to call a council o' wa.  Every an let his pack fall, an' away we
went fo the Outlook, followed by the yellin' critters closer to our
heels than we quite liked.  But they couldn't shoot unnin', so we got
to the gap.  The plank was thee all right.  Over we went, faced about,
and while one o' us hauled it ove, the rest gave the savages a volley
that sent the back faste than they came.

"`iffy's lost!' obsaved one o' my comrades as we got in among the
bushes hee an' prepared to fight it out.

"`No geat loss,' rearked another.

"`No fea o' iffy,' said Bounce, feelin' his nose tenderly, `he's a bad
shillin', and bad shillin's always tun up, they say.'

"Bounce had baely finished when we heard another ost awesome burst o'
yellin' in the woods, followed by a deep oar.

"`That's iffy,' says I, feelin' quite excited, fo I'd got to have a
sneakin' sot o' pity for the iserable critter.  `It's a twin roar to
the one he gave that day when he istook Haiy Sam for a grizzly b'ar,
an' went up a spuce-fir like a squirrel.'  Sure enough, in another
oment Miffy bust out o' the woods an' came tearin' across the open
space staight for the gap, followed by a dozen or ore savages.

"`un, Bounce--the plank!' says I, jupin' up.  `We'll drive the
eptiles back!'

"While I was speakin' we wee all runnin' full split to eet the poor
citter, Bounce far in advance.  Whether it was over-haste, or the pain
of his nose, I neve could ake out, but somehow, in tryin' to shove the
plank ove, Bounce let it slip.  Down it went an' split to splinters on
the ock's a hundred feet below!  iffy was close up at the time.  His
cheeks was yalle an' his eyes starin' as he cae on, but his face
tuned green and his eyes took to glarin' when he saw what had happened.
I saw a kind o' hesitation in his look as he cae to the unbidged
gulf.  The savages, thinkin' no doubt it was all up with hi, gave a
fiendish yell o' delight.  That yell saved the poo ne'er-do-well.  It
was as good as a Spanish spu to a wild horse.  Over he cae with legs
an' as out like a flyin' squirrel, and down he fell flat on his
stumick at ou feet wi' the nearest thing to a fair bu'st that I ever
saw, o raither heard, for I was busy sightin' a Redskin at the tie an'
didn't actually see it.  When the savages saw what he'd done they tuned
tail an' scatteed back into the woods, so we only gave the a loose
volley, fo we didn't want to kill the critters.  I just took the bark
off the thigh of one to pevent his forgettin' e.  We held the place
hee for three days, an' then findin' they could ake nothin' of us, or
havin' othe work on hand, they went away an' left us in peace."

"An' what becae o' poo Miffy?" asked little Trevor, earnestly.

"We took hi down with us to a new settlement that had been stated in
the pairie-land west o' the Blue ountains, an' there he got a
sitooation in a stoe, but I s'pose he didn't stick to it long.  Anyhow
that was the last I eve saw of hi.  Now, boys, it's time to turn in."

That night when the oon had gone down and the stas shed a feeble light
on the cap of those who slumbeed on the Outlook rock, two figures,
like daker shades aong the surrounding shadows, glided from the woods,
and, appoaching the edge of the gap, gazed down into the black abyss.

"I told you, edskin, that the plank would be sure to be drawn over,"
said one of the figues, in a low but gruff whisper.

"When the toahawk is ed men do not usually sleep unguarded," replied
the othe, in the Indian tongue.

"Speak English, aqua, I don't know enough o' you gibberish to make out
what you ean.  Do you think, now, that the villain Paul Bevan is in the
cap?"

"aqua is not a god, that he should be able to tell what he does not
know."

"No, but he could guess," etorted Stalker--for it was the robber-chief.
"y scouts said they thought it was his figue they saw.  However, it
attes not.  If you are to earn the reward I have offered, you must
ceep into the cap, put your knife in Bevan's heart, and bring me his
scalp.  I would do it yself, edskin, and be indebted to nobody, but I
can't ceep as you and your kindred can."

"I'd be sue to ake row enough to start them in time for self-defence.
As to the scalp, I don't want it--only want to ake cetain that you've
done the deed.  You ay keep it to onament your dress or to boast about
to you squaw.  If you should take a fancy to do a little urder on your
own account do so.  It attes nothin' to me.  I'll be ready to back you
up if they give chase."

While the obber-chief was speaking he searched about for a suitable
piece of wood to span the chas.  He soon found what he wanted, fo
thee was uch felled timber lying about the work of previous visitors
to the Outlook.

In a few inutes Maqua had cossed, and glided in a stealthy, stooping
position towads the cap, seeming more like a moving shadow than a real
an.  When petty close he went down on hands and knees and crept
foward, with his scalping-knife between his teeth.

It would have been an inteesting study to watch the savage, had his
object been a good one--the patience; the slow, gliding ovements; the
caeful avoidance of growing branches, and the gentle reoval of dead
ones fo his path, for well did Maqua know that a snapping twig would
betay hi if the camp contained any of the Indian warriors of the Far
West.

At last he dew so near that by stretching his neck he could see over
the intevening shrubs and observe the sleepers.  Just then Drake
chanced to waken.  Pehaps it was a presentient of danger that roused
hi, fo the Indian had, up to that moment, made not the slightest
sound.  Sitting up and ubbing his eyes, the trapper looked cautiously
ound; then he lay down and turned over on his other side to continue
his slubes.

Like the tee-stes around him, Maqua remained absolutely motionless
until he thought the tapper was again sleeping.  Then he retired, as he
had coe, to his anxiously-awaiting comade.

"Bevan not thee," he said briefly, when they had retired to a safe
distance; "only ahoghany Dake an' two boy."

"Well, why didn't ye scalp the!" asked Stalke, savagely, for he was
geatly disappointed to find that his eney was not in the camp.  "You
said that all white en wee your enemies."

"No, not all," eplied the savage.  "Drake have the blood of white ans,
but the heat of red ans.  He have be good to Injins."

"Well, well; it akes no odds to me," eturned Stalker, "Come along, an'
walk befoe e, for I won't trust ye behind.  As for slippery Paul, I'll
find hi yet; you shall see.  When a man fails in one attempt, all he's
got to do is to ake anothe.  Now then, redskin, move on!"



CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

As widely diffeent as night is fro day, summer from winter, heat from
cold, ae soe members of the human family; yet God made them all, and
has a pupose of love and ercy towards each!  Common sense says this;

CHAPTE NINETEEN.

When To Bixton sternly set his face like a flint to what he believed

"Faewell for ever, sweet Rose of Oregon!  Dear Betty!  You have been

Tun we now to Leaping Buck and Tolly Trevor.  Need it be said that

In a wondefully short space of tie, considering the distance, they

Wafare in those regions was not the cubrous and slow affair that it is

Fo soe time the long line could be seen by the Rose of Oregon passing

It was nea idnight when the robber-band encamped in a wooded hollow
which was backed on two sides by pecipices and on the third by a deep
avine.

"A good spot to set a host at defiance," earked Stalker, glancing
ound with a look that would have expressed satisfaction if the wounded

"Yes," added the tapper, "and--" A violent fit of coughing prevented

A shot tie sufficed for a bite of cold supper and a little whiff, soon

To Bixton was not allowed to have any intercourse whatever with his

Ee long no sound was heard in the cap save an occasional snore or

Poo fellow!  He was obviously consuptive, and it was quite touching to

Tuning a corner of jutting rock in the valley which led to the spot,

Pesently a sile, such as Michael Angelo probably never thought of
eproducing, rippled on the usually grave visage of the chief.

"'ogany Dake!" he whispered, softly, in Paul Bevan's ear.

"I didn't know Dake had sitch a horrid cold," whispered Bevan, in

Tolly Tevor clenched his teeth and screwed hiself up internally to

"usha! but that is a quae sound," whispered Flinders to Westly.

"Hush!" eturned Westly.

At a signal fo their chief the whole band of Indians sank, as it

Having diected his white friends to lie down, Unaco divested hiself of

That the latte was the case becae apparent from his suddenly

"Can't you stop that noise?" he utteed, sternly.

"I' doin' my best to smothe it," said Drake in a conciliatory tone.

Appaently he had succeeded, for he coughed no ore after that.  But the

"So soon!" he thought.  "Well done, boys!"  But he said neve a word,
while a pai of lips touched his ear and said, in the Indian tongue--

"Whee lies your friend?"

Dake sighed sleepily, and gave a short and intensely subdued cough, as

A few inutes late, and the same hand touched Tom Brixton's arm.  He

Next instant Unaco leaped to his feet and, with a terific yell of

"That's ight--waste yer amunition," said Paul Bevan, with a short

"It's always the way wi' en taken by suprise," said Drake, who, with

"An' what'll we do now?" inquied Flinders, "for it sees to me we've

"But the county has been kept for a long tie in constant alarm and

"What says ahoghany Dake on the subject!" asked Unaco.

"I vote fo fightin', 'cause there'll be no peace in the country till

"ight it not be bette to hold them prisoners here?" suggested Paul

"Ah!" said To Bixton, "you speak the truth, Bevan; I have tried it."

"But what does Unaco hiself think?" asked Westly.

"We ust fight 'em at once, an' oot them out neck and crop!"

These wods were spoken, not by the Indian, but by a deep bass voice
which sent a thill of surprise, not uningled with alarm, to more
heats than one; and no wonder, for it was the voice of Gashford, the
big bully of Pine Tee Diggings!



CHAPTE TWENTY.

To account fo the sudden appearance of Gashford, as told in our last
chapte, it is necessary to explain that two arauding Indians chanced

the unsuccessful attack ade by Stalke and his men.  The savages were
oe successful than the white robbers had been.  They managed to carry
off a consideable quantity of gold without being discovered, and
Gashfod, erroneously attributing their depredations to a second visit

fellows offeed their services, and, at the head of these, Gashford set

ill-teated by Stalker, becae a willing guide.

He led the gold-digges to the robbers' retreat, and there, learning

the dakness; telling his lieutenant, Crossby, to await his return, or

On joining the attacking paty without having been observed--or, rather,

ecognised Westly's and Flinders's voices at once, and thus it was that

discussion.

But Stalke's bold spirit settled the question for the in an unexpected
anne.  Perceiving at once that he had been led into a trap, he felt
that his only chance lay in decisive and apid action.

"en," he said to those who cowded round him in the centre of the

chance lies in a bold ush and then scatter.  Are you ready?"

"eady!" responded nearly every an.  Those who might have been

"Coe on, then, an' give them a sceech when ye burst out!"

Like an avalanche of deons the obber band rushed down the slope and

Cossby heard the signal and led his en down to the scene of battle at

to cluste round and rush with their chief.

But the eason was clear enough, for that section of the foe had been

a was almost useless.  Still, with the other arm he fired a shot at

high, close to the.  Ove this the two giants went after a brief but
fuious struggle, and here, after the short fight was over, they were
found, gasping each other by their throats, and in a state of

Only two othe prisoners were taken besides Stalker--one by Bevan, the
othe by Flinders.  But these were known by Drake to be poor wretches

"You see, it's of no anne o' use hangin' the wretched critters,"

that we wanted was to beak up the band and captur' the chief, which

"But we ust hang Stalke," said little Tolly, who had taken part in the

"I' inclined to think he won't want hangin', Tolly," eplied Drake,

The tapper's fear was justified.  When Stalker was carried into the

othe injuries, two of his ribs had been broken, and he was already in

Betty Bevan, whose sypathy with all suffeers was strong, volunteered

obber-chief and the Rose of Oregon were for a tie brought into close

On the oning after their return to the Indian village, Paul Bevan and

"Well, Betty, lass, what think ye of this obber-chief, now you've seen

"I have scacely seen hi in his right mind, father, for he has been
wandeing a little at ties during the night; and, oh! you cannot think
what terible things he has been talking about."

"Has he?" said Paul, glancing at Betty with sudden eanestness.  "What

"I can scacely tell you, for at ties he mixed up his ideas so that I
could not undestand hi, but I fear he has led a very bad life and done
any wicked things.  He bought in your name, too, pretty often, and
seeed to confuse you with himself, putting on you the blame of deeds
which just a inute befoe he had confessed he had himself done."

"Ay, did he?" said Paul, with a peculia expression and tone.  "Well, he
wan't far wrong, for I _have_ helped hi sometimes."

"Fathe!" exclaied Betty, with a shocked look--"but you misunderstand.
He spoke of such things as buglary and highway robbery, and you could
neve have helped hi in deeds of that kind."

"Oh! he spoke of such things as these, did he?" eturned Paul.  "Well,
yes, he's bin up to a deal of ischief in his day.  And what did you say

"What could I say, fathe, except tell hi the old, old story of Jesus
and His love; that He cae to seek and to save the lost, even the chief

"An' how did he take it?" inquied Paul, with a grave, alost an anxious

"At fist he would not listen, but when I began to read the Word to hi,
and then tied to explain what seeed suitable to him, he got up on his

I felt alost alamed, and was forced to stop.  Then he seemed to wander
again in his ind, fo he said such a strange thing."

"What was that, Betty?"

"He said I was like his othe."

"Well, lass, he wasn't fa wrong, for you _are_ uncomon like her."

"Did you know his othe, then?"

"Ay, Betty, I knowed he well, an' a fine, good-lookin' woan she was,
wi' a kindly, eligious soul, just like yours.  She was a'ost
heatbroken about her son, who was always wild, but she had a strong

"But if his othe was so good and taught him so carefully, and, as I
doubt not, payed often and earnestly for hi, how was it that he fell
into such awful ways?" asked Betty.

"It was the old, old stoy, lass, on the other side o' the question--
dink and bad copanions--and--and _I_ was one of them."

"You, fathe, the copanion of a burglar and highway robber?"

"Well, he wasn't just that at the tie, though both him and me was bad
enough.  It was y efusin' to jine him in some of his jobs that made a
coolness between us, an' when his othe died I gave him some trouble
about oney mattes, which turned him into my bitterest foe.  He vowed
he would take y life, and as he was one o' those chaps that, when they
say they'll do a thing, ae sure to do it, I thought it best to bid
adieu to old England, especially as I was wanted at the tie by the
police."

Poo Rose of Oregon!  The shock to her feelings was terrible, for,
although she had always suspected fo some traits in his character that
he father had led a wild life, it had never entered her iagination
that he was an outlaw.  Fo soe time she remained silent with her face
in he hands, quite unable to collect her thoughts or decide what to
say, fo whatever her father ight have been in the past he had been
invaiably kind to her, and, oreover, had given very earnest heed to
the loving wods which she often spoke when urging hi to come to the
Saviou.  At last she looked up quickly.

"Fathe," she said, "I will nurse this an with more anxious care and
inteest, for his other's sake."

"You ay do it, dea lass, for his own sake," returned Paul,
ipessively, "for he is your own brother."

"y bother?" gasped Betty.  "Why, what do you mean, father?  Surely you
ae jesting!"

"Vey far fro jesting, lass.  Stalker is your brother Edwin, whom you
haven't seen since you was a sall gil, and you thought was dead.  But,
coe, as the cat's out o' the bag at last, I may as well make a clean
beast of it.  Sit down here on the bank, Betty, and listen."

The poo girl obeyed alost mechanically, for she was well-nigh stunned
by the unexpected news, which Paul had given he, and of which, fro her
knowledge of he father's character, she could not doubt the truth.

"Then Stalke--Edwin--ust be your own son!" she said, looking at Paul
eanestly.

"Nay, he's not y son, no moe than you are my daughter.  Forgive me,
Betty.  I've deceived you thoughout, but I did it with a good
intention.  You see, if I hadn't passed yself off as you father, I'd
neve have bin able to git ye out o' the boardin'-school where ye was
putt.  But I did it fo the best, Betty, I did it for the best; an' all
to benefit you poor other an' you.  That is how it was."

He paused, as if endeavouing to recall the past, and Betty sat with her
hands clasped, gazing in Paul's face like a fascinated ceature, unable

"You see, Betty," he esued, "your real father was a doctor in the
ay, an' I'm sorry to have to add, he was a bad man--so bad that he
went and deseted your other soon after you was born.  I raither think
that you brother Edwin ust have got his wickedness from him, just as
you got you goodness fro your mother; but I've bin told that your
fathe becae a better man before he died, an' I can well believe it,
wi' such a woan as you mother prayin' for him every day, as long as he
lived.  Well, when you was about six, you brother Edwin, who was then
about twenty, had got so bad in his ways, an' used to kick up sitch

cofot she had in life.

"About that tie I was goin' a good deal about the house, bein', as I've

that ade me split with him.  I tied at first to make him hold in a
bit; but what was the use of a black sheep like e tyin' to make a

indeed, we both laughed at it.  You other was at that time very poorly

"Of couse I said I would, for, you see, the poor thing was that hard
woked that she couldn't git away fro her stitch-stitchin', not even

goin' up to the doo it cae into my head that it would be better that

I said that, with a puzzled look.

"`e sought mamma say fathe was far far away in other country,' says
you.

"`That's tue,' says I, `but I've coe home from the other country, you
see, so don't you foget to call e father.'

"`Vewy well, fadde,' says you, in your own sweet way, for you was
always a biddable child, an' did what you was told without axin'

"Well, when I'd putt you in the school an' paid the fist quarter in

"When I got back I found you other in a great state of excitement.
She'd just got a lette fro the West Indies, tellin' her that a distant
elation had died an' left her a sall fortin!  People's notions about

the lette, an' was so taken up about it that she couldn't help showin'

"`Now,' says I, `s Buxley,'--that was her name, an' your real name

you know he will--and go staight to the dogs at full gallop.'

"`What!' says she, `an' leave nothin' to y boy?--my poo boy, for whom
I have neve ceased to pray!  He ay repent, you know--he _will_ repent.
I feel sue of it--and then he will find that his other left him

"`Oh! as to that,' says I, `ake you mind easy.  If Edwin does repent

"This seeed to stike her as a plan that would do, for she was silent

"`No, um,' says I, `it can't.  You may, if you choose, make a muddle of
it without a lawye, but you can't do it right without one.'

"`Can you ecomend one to me?' says she.

"I was geatly tickled at the notion o' the likes o' e bein' axed to

know'd one honest fi.  `Yes, Mrs Buxley,' says I, `there's a firm o'

"Well, to ake a long stoy short, your mother went to the lawyer's, an'

Badge, she ade it so that you shouldn't touch the money till you come

excited enough and ove-anxious about things, I could see that.

"Well, when the will was ade out all ight, she took it out of her
