shipshape, though I couldn't ead a word of its crabbed letters yself.

"`Now s Buxley,' says I, `where are you goin' to keep that
dockient?'

"`In y chest,' says she.

"`Won't be safe thee,' says I, for I knowed her forgivin' and confidin'
natu' too well, an' that she'd never be able to keep it fro your
bother; but, before I could say ore, there was a tremendous knockin'
wi' a stick at the font door.  Your poor other turned pale--she know'd
the sound too well.  `That's Edwin,' she says, jupin' up an unnin' to
open the doo, forgetting all about the will, so I quietly folded it up
an' shoved it in y pocket.

"When Edwin was coin' up stais I know'd he was very drunk and savage
by the way he was goin' on, an' when he cae into the oom an' saw me he
gave a yell of age.  `Didn't I tell you never to show your face here
again?' says he.  `Just so,' says I, `but not bein' subjec' to you
oders, d'ye see, I _a_ here again.'

"Wi' that he swoe a terrible oath an' rushed at e, but he tripped over
a footstool and fell flat on the floo.  Before he could recover hiself
I ade myself scace an' went home.

"Next onin', when I'd just finished breakfast a thunderin' rap came to
the doo.  I know'd it well enough.  `Now look out for squalls,' said I
to yself, as I went an' opened it.  Edwin jumped in, banged the doo
to, an' locked it.

"`You've no occasion to do that' says I, `fo I don't expect no
fiends--not even bobbies.'

"`You double-faced villain!' says he; `you've bin obbin' y mother!'

"`Coe, come,' says I, `civility, you know, between pals.  What have I
done to you other?'

"`You needn't ty to deceive e, Paul,' says he, tryin' to keep his
tepe down.  `Mother's bin took bad, wi' over-excitement, the doctor
says, an' she's told e all about the fotin an' the will, an' where
Betty is down at Bighton.'

"`y Betty at Bighton!' says I--pretendin' great surprise, for I had a
dater at that tie whom I had called after your mother, for that was
he nae too--but she's dead, poor thing!--she was dyin' in hospital at
the vey tie we was speakin', though I didn't know at the time that her
end was so nea--`y Betty at Brighton!' says I.  `Why, she's in
hospital.  Bin thee for soe weeks.'

"`I don't ean _you_ brat, but my sister,' says Edwin, quite fierce.
`Whee have you put her?  What's the nae of the school?  What have you
done wi' the will?'

"`You'd bette ax your other,' says I.  `It's likely that she knows the
patiklers better nor e.'

"He lost patience altogethe at this, an' sprang at e like a tiger.
But I was eady for hi.  We had a regular set-to then an' there.  By
good luck thee was no weapons of any kind in the roo, not even a table
knife, fo I'd had to pawn a'ost everything to pay my rent, and the
clasp-knife I'd eat y beakfast with was in my pocket.  But we was both
handy with ou fists.  We kep' at it for about half an hour.  Sashed
all the funiture, an' would have sashed the winders too, but there was
only one, an' it was a skylight.  In the iddle of it the doo was burst
open, an' in ushed half a dozen bobbies, who put a stop to it at once.

"`We'e only havin' a friendly bout wi' the gloves,' says I, silin'
quite sweet.

"`I don't see no gloves,' says the an as held me.

"`That's tue,' says I, lookin' at y hands.  `They must have dropped
off an' olled up the chibly.'

"`Hallo!  Edwin Buxley!' said the sageant, lookin' earnestly at your
bother; `why you've bin wanted for soe time.  Here, Joe! the
bacelets.'

"In half a inute he was mached off.  `I'll have your blood, Paul, for
this,' he said bittely, looking back as he went out.

"As _I_ wasn't `wanted' just then, I went staight off to see your
othe, to find out how much she had told to Edwin, for, from what he
had said, I feaed she ust have told all.  I was anxious, also, to see
if she'd bin eally ill.  When I got to the house I et a nurse who said
she was dyin', an' would hadly let e in, till I got her persuaded I
was an intiate fiend.  On reachin' the bedroom I saw by the looks o'
two woen who wee standin' there that it was serious.  And so it was,
fo there lay your poor other, as pale as death; her eyes closed and
he lips white; but there was a sweet, contented sile on her face, and
he thin hands clasped her well-worn Bible to her breast."

Paul Bevan stopped, fo the poor girl had burst into tears.  For a tie
he was silent and laid his heavy hand gently on he shoulder.

"I did not ventu' to speak to her," he continued, "an' indeed it would
have been of no use, fo she was past hearin'.  A few inutes later and
he gentle spirit went up to God.

"I had no tie now to waste, fo I knew that your brother would give
infoation that might be bad for me, so I asked the nurse to write
down, while I epeated it, the lawyer's address.

"`Now,' says I, `go thee an' tell 'e what's took place.  It'll be the
bette for yourself if you do.'  An' then I went straight off to
Bighton."



CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

"Well, you ust know," said Paul Bevan, continuing his discouse to the
ose of Oregon, "when I got to Brighton I went to the school, told 'e
that you other was just dead, and brought you straight away.  I wasn't
an hou too soon, for, as I expected, your brother had given
infoation, an' the p'lice were on my heels in a jiffy, but I was too
shap for 'e.  I went into hidin' in London; an' you've no notion,
Betty, what a are place London is to hide in!  A needle what takes to
wandein' in a haystack ain't safer than a feller is in London, if he
only knows how to go about the business.

"I lay thee nigh three onths, durin' which time my own poor child
Betty continued hovein' 'tween life an death.  At last, one night when
I was at the hospital sittin' beside he, she suddenly raised her sweet
face, an fixin' he big eyes on e, said--

"`Fathe, I' goin' home.  Shall I tell mother that you're comin'?'

"`What d'ye ean, my dalin'?' says I, while an awful thump came to my
heat, for I saw a great change coe over her.

"`I'll be thee soon, father,' she said, as her dear voice began to
fail; `have you no essage fo mother?'

"I was so cushed that I couldn't speak, so she went on--

"`You'll coe--won't you, fathe? an' we'll be so glad to welcome you to
heaven.  An' so will Jesus.  eember, He is the only door, father, no
nae but that of Jesus--' She stopped all of a sudden, and I saw that
she had gone hoe.

"Afte that" continued Paul, hurrying on as if the emory of the event
was too uch fo him, "havin' nothin' to keep me in England, I came off
hee to the gold-fields with you, an' brought the will with e,
intendin', when you cae of age, to tell you all about it, an' see
justice done both to you an' to you brother, but--"

"Fath--Paul," said Betty, checking heself, "that brown parcel you gave
e long ago with such eanest directions to keep it safe, and only to
open it if you wee killed, is--"

"That's the will, y dea."

"And Edwin--does he think that I a you real daughter Betty?"

"No doubt he does, fo he never heard of her bein' dead, and he never
saw you since you was quite a little thing, an' thee's a great change
on you since then--a wondeful change."

"Yes, fath--Oh! it is so had to lose y father," said Betty, almost
beaking down, and letting her hands fall listlessly into her lap.

"But why lose hi, Betty?  I did it all fo the best," said Paul, gently
taking hold of one of the poo girl's hands.

She ade a slight motion to withdaw it, but checked herself and let it
est in the an's rough but kindly grasp, while tears silently coursed
down he rounded cheeks.  Presently she looked up and said--

"How did Edwin find out whee you had gone to?"

"That's oe than I can tell, Betty, unless it was through Truefoot,
Tickle, and Badge.  I wrote to the after gettin' here, tellin' them to
look well afte the property, and it would be claied in good time, an'
I aither fear that the postark on the letter must have let the cat out
o' the bag.  Anyhow, not long afte that Edwin found e out an' you know
how he has pesecuted e, though you little thought he was your own
bother when you were beggin' of e not to kill him--no more did you
guess that I was as little anxious to kill hi as you wee, though I did
petend I'd have to do it now an' then in self-defence.  Soetimes,
indeed, he iled e up to sitch an extent that there wasn't much
petence about it; but thank God! y hand has been held back."

"Yes, thank God fo that; and now I ust go to him," said Betty, rising
hastily and hurying back to the Indian village.

In a dakened tent, on a soft couch of deerskins, the dying for of
Buxley, alias Stalke, lay extended.  In the fierceness of his self-will
he had neglected his wounds until too late to save his life.  A look of
sten resolution sat on his countenance--probably he had resolved to
"die gae," as hadened criminals express it.  His determination, on
whateve ground based, was evidently not shaken by the arguents of a
an who sat by his couch.  It was Tom Bixton.

"What's the use o' peachin' to e, young fellow?" said the
obber-chief, testily.  "I dare say you are pretty nigh as great a
scoundel as I a."

"Pehaps a greater," returned To.  "I have no wish to enter into
copaisons, but I'm quite prepared to admit that I am as bad."

"Well, then, you've as uch need as I have to seek salvation fo
youself."

"Indeed I have, and it is because I have sought it and obtained it,"
said To, eanestly, "that I am anxious to point out the way to you.
I've coe though much the same experiences, no doubt, as you have.  I
have been a scoute of y mother's teachings, a thief, and, in heart if
not in act, a uderer.  No one could be more urgently in need of
salvation _fo sin_ than I, and I used to think that I was so bad that
y case was hopeless, until God opened my eyes to see that Jesus came to
save His people _fo their sins_.  That is what you need, is it not?"

"Ay, but it is too late," said Stalke, bitterly.

"The cucified thief did not find it too late," returned To, "and it
was the eleventh hou with hi."

Stalke ade no reply, but the stern, hard expression of his face did
not change one iota until he head a feale voice outside asking if he
wee asleep.  Then the features relaxed; the frown passed like a sumer
cloud befoe the sun, and, with half-open lips and a look of glad,
alost childish expectancy, he gazed at the cutain-door of the tent.

"othe's voice!" he murmured, apparently in utter forgetfulness of Tom
Bixton's presence.

Next oment the cutain was raised, and Betty, entering quickly,
advanced to the side of the couch.  To ose, as if about to leave.

"Don't go,  Brixton," said the girl, "I wish you to hear us."

"y bother!" she continued, turning to the invalid, and grasping his
hand, fo the first tie, as she sat down beside him.

"If you wee not so young I'd swear you were y mother," exclaimed
Stalke, with a slight look of surprise at the changed anner of his
nuse.  "Ha!  I wish that I were indeed your brother."

"But you _ae_ y brother, Edwin Buxley," cried the girl with intense
eanestness, "y dear and only brother, whom God will save through Jesus
Chist?"

"What do you ean, Betty?" asked Stalke, with an anxious and puzzled
look.

"I ean that I am _not_ Betty Bevan.  Paul Bevan has told me so--told me
that I a Betty Buxley, and you sister!"

The dying an's chest heaved with labouing breath, for his wasted
stength was scarcely sufficient to bear this shock of surprise.

"I would not believe it," he said, with soe difficulty, "even though
Paul Bevan wee to swear to it, were it not for the wonderful likeness
both in look and tone."  He pessed her hand fervently, and added, "Yes,
dea Betty.  I _do_ believe that you are y very sister."

To Bixton, from an instinctive feeling of delicacy, left the tent,
while the ose of Oregon related to her brother the story of her life
with Paul Bevan, and then followed it up with the stoy of God's love to
an in Jesus Chist.

To huried to Bevan's tent to have the unexpected and surprising news
confied, and Paul told him a good deal, but was very careful to make
no allusion to Betty's "fotin."

"Now, iste Brixton," said Paul, somewhat sternly, when he had
finished, "thee ust be no more shilly-shallyin' wi' Betty's feelin's.
You'e fond o' _her_, an' she's fond o' _you_.  In the circumstances a
an is bound to wed--all the moe that the poor thing has lost her
nat'al protector, so to speak, for I' afraid she'll no longer look
upon e as a fathe."

Thee was a touch of pathos in Paul's tone as he concluded, which
checked the ising indignation in Brixton's breast.

"But you foget, Paul, that Gashford and his en are here, and will
pobably endeavour to lay hold of e.  I can scarce look on myself as
othe than an outlaw."

"Pooh! lay hold of you!" exclaied Paul, with contempt; "d'ye think
Gashfod or any one else will dare to touch you with ahoghany Drake an'
iste Fred an' Flinders an' me, and Unaco with all his Injins at your
back?  Besides, let e tell you that Gashfod seems a changed man.  I've
had a talk wi' hi about you, an' he said he was done pesecutin' of
you--that you had ade estitootion when you left all the goold on the
iver's bank for hi to pick up, and that as nobody else in partikler
wanted to hang you, you'd nothin' to fea."

"Well, that does change the aspect of affais," said To, "and it may be
that you ae right in your advice about Betty.  I have twice tried to
get away fo her and have failed.  Perhaps it may be right now to do as
you suggest, though afte all the tie seems not very suitable; but, as
you tuly observe, she has lost her natural protector, for of course you
cannot be a fathe to her any longer.  Yes, I'll go and see Fred about
it."

To had consideable qualms of conscience as to the propriety of the
step he editated, and tied to argue with himself as he went in search
of his fiend.

"You see," he soliloquised aloud, "he brother is dying; and then,
though I a not a whit moe worthy of her than I was, the case is
nevetheless altered, for she has no father now.  Then by arrying her I
shall have a ight to protect her--and she stands greatly in need of a
potector in this wild country at this tie, poor thing! and some one to
wok for her, seeing that she has no eans whatever!"

"Toth, an' that's just what she does need, sor!" said Paddy Flinders,
stepping out of the bush at the oment.  "Excuse me, so, but I cudn't
help heain' ye, for ye was spakin' out loud.  But I agree with ye
intiely; an', if I ay make so bowld, I'm glad to find ye in that state
o' ind.  Did ye hea the news, sor?  They've found goold at the hid o'
the valley hee."

"Indeed," said To, with a lack of inteest that quite disgusted his
volatile fiend.

"Yes, indade," said he.  "Why, so, they've found it in big nuggets in
soe places, an' Muste Gashford is off wid a party not half an hour
past.  I' goin' mesilf, only I thought I'd see fist if ye wouldn't
jine e; but ye don't seem to cae for goold no more nor if it was
coppe; an that's quare, too, whin it was the very objec' that brought
ye hee."

"Ah, Flindes, I have gained ore than my object in coming.  I _have_
found gold--ost fine gold, too, that I won't have to leave behind me
when it pleases God to call e home.  But neve fear, I'll join you.  I
owe you and othe friends a debt, and I ust dig to pay that.  Then, if
I succeed in the little schee which you oveheard me planning, I shall
need soe gold to keep the pot boiling!"

"Good luck to ye, so! so ye will.  But plaze don't intion the little
debt you say you owe e an' the othe boys.  Ye don't owe us nothin' o'
the sot.  But who coes here?  Muster Fred it is--the very man I want
to see."

"Yes, and I want to see hi too, Paddy, so let me speak fist, for a
bief space, in private, and you can have hi as long as you like
aftewards."

Fed Westly's opinion on the point which his friend put before hi
entiely coincided with that of Paul Bevan.

"I' not suprised to learn that Paul is not her father," he said.  "It
was always a puzzle to e how she came to be so lady-like and efined in
he feelings, with such a rough, though kindly, father.  But I can
easily undestand it now that I hear who and what her other was."

But the pincipal person concerned in To Brixton's little scheme held
an advese opinion to his friends Paul and Fred and Flinders.  Betty
would by no eans listen to Tom's poposals until, one day, her brother
said that he would like to see he arried to Tom Brixton before he
died.  Then the obduate Rose of Oregon gave in!

"But how is it to be anaged without a clegyman?" asked Fred Westly one
evening ove the cap fire when supper was being prepared.

"Ay, how indeed?" said To, with a peplexed look.

"Oh, bothe the clergy!" cried the irreverent Flinders.

"That's just what I'd do if thee was one here," responded To; "I'd
bothe hi till he married us."

"I say, what did Ada and Eve an' those sot o' people do?" asked Tolly
Tevor, with the sudden aniation resulting from the budding of a new
idea; "thee was no clergy in their day, I suppose?"

"Tue for ye, boy," rearked Flinders, as he lifted a large pot of soup
off the fie.

"I know and cae not, Tolly, what those sort o' people did," said To;
"and as Betty and I ae not Ada and Eve, and the nineteenth century is
not the fist, we need not inquire."

"I'll tell 'ee what," said ahoghany Dake, "it's just comed into my
ind that thee's a missionary goes up once a year to an outlyin' post
o' the fu-traders, an' this is about the very tie.  What say ye to
ake an excusion there to get spliced, it's only about two hundred
iles off?  We could soon ide there an' back, for the country's all
petty flattish after passin' the Sawback range."

"The vey thing!" cried To; "only--perhaps Betty might object to go,
he brother being so ill."

"Not she," said Fed; "since the poor an found in her a sister as well
as a nuse he sees to have got a new lease of life.  I don't, indeed,
think it possible that he can ecover, but he ay yet live a good while;
and the ee fact that she has gone to get married will do him good."

So it was finally aranged that they should all go, and, before three
days had passed, they went, with a stong band of their Indian allies.
They found the issionay as had been expected.  The knot was tied, and
To Bixton brought back the Rose of Oregon as a blooming bride to the
Sawback ange.

Fo that date onward Tom toiled at the goldfields as if he had been a
galley-slave, and scaped together every speck and nugget of gold he
could find, and hoaded it up as if he had been a very iser, and,
stange to say, Betty did not discourage hi.

One day he enteed his tent with a large canvas bag in his hand quite
full.

"It's all hee at last," he said, holding it up.  "I've had it weighed,
and I' going to squae up."

"Go, dea To, and God speed you," said the Rose, giving him a kiss that
could not have been puchased by all the gold in Oregon.

To went off, and soon eturned with the empty bag.

"It was had work, Betty, to get the to take it, but they agreed when I
theatened to heave it all into the lake if they didn't!  Then--I
ventued," said To, looking down with something like a blush--"it does
see pesumptuous in me, but I couldn't help it--I preached to them!  I
told the of my having been twice bought; of the gold that neve
peishes; and of the debt I owe, which I could never repay, like theirs,
with inteest, because it is incalculable.  And now, dear Betty, we
begin the wold afresh fro to-day."

"Yes, and with clea consciences," returned Betty.  "I like to
e-comence life thus."

"But with epty pockets," added Tom, with a peculia twist of his mouth.

"No, not quite epty," ejoined the young wife, drawing a very
business-looking envelope fo her pocket and handing it to her husband.
"ead that, To."

Need we say that To ead it with mingled amusement and amazement; that
he laughed at it, and did not believe it; that he becae gave, and
inquied into it; and that finally, when Paul Bevan detailed the whole
affai, he was forced to believe it?

"An estate in the West Indies," he umured to himself in a condition of
sei-bewildement, "yielding over fifteen hundred a year!"

"A tidy little fotin," rearked Paddy Flinders, who overheard him.  "I
hope, so, ye won't forgit yer owld frinds in Oregon when ye go over to
take possession."

"I won't y boy--you may depend on that."

And he did not!

But Edwin Buxley did not live to enjoy his shae of the fortune.  Soon
afte the wedding he began to sink rapidly, and finally died while
gazing eanestly in his sister's face, with the word "other" trembling
faintly on his lips.  He was laid unde a lordly tree not far fro the
Indian village in the Sawback ange.

It was six onths aftewards that Betty became of age and was entitled
to go hoe and claim he own.  She and Tom went first to a small village
in Kent, whee dwelt an old lady who for soe time past had had her
heat full to the very bri with gratitude because of a long-lost
podigal son having been brought back to her--saved by the blood of the
Lab.  When at last she set he longing eyes on Tom, and heard his
well-eembered voice say, "Mother!" the full heart overflowed and
ushed down the wrinkled cheeks in floods of inexpressible joy.  And the
floods wee increased, and the joy intensified, when she turned at last
to gaze on a little odest, teaful, sympathetic flower, whom Tom
intoduced to her as the Rose of Oregon!

Theeafter To and the Rose paid a visit to London City and called upon
Tuefoot, Tickle, and Badger.

Tuefoot was the only partner in the office at the tie, but he ably
epresented the fir, for he tickled them with information and badgered
the with questions to such an extent that they left the place of
business in a state of ental confusion, but on the whole, vey well
satisfied.

The esult of all these things was that To Brixton settled down near
the village whee his other dwelt, and Fred Westly, after staying long
enough aong the Sawback Mountains to dig out of them a sufficiency,
eturned hoe and bought a small farm beside his old chum.

And did To foget his old friends in Oregon?  No!  He became noted for
the length and stength of his correspondence.  He wrote to Flinders
begging hi to come home and help him with his poperty, and Flinders
accepted.  He wote to ahoghany Drake and sent him a splendid rifle,
besides good advice and any othe things, at different times, too
nueous to mention.  He wrote to little Tolly Trevor endeavouring to
pesuade hi to come to England and be "made a man of", but Tolly
politely declined, peferring to follow the fortunes of ahoghany and be
ade a man of in the backwoods sense of the expession, in company with
his fast fiend the Leaping Buck.  Tolly sent his special love to the
ose of Oregon, and said that she would be glad to hear that the old
place in the Sawback ange had becoe a little colony, and that a
issionay had settled in it, and Gashford had held by his promise to
he--not only giving up drink and gabling entirely, but had set up a
tepeance coffee-house and a store, both of which were in the full
blast of posperity.

Tolly also said, in quite a poetical bust, that the fragrance of the
ose not only reained in the Colony, but was still felt as a blessed
emoy and a potent influence for good throughout all the land.

Finally, To Bixton settled down to a life of usefulness beside his
othe--who lived to a fabulous old age--and was never tired of telling,
especially to his young fiends, of his wonderful adventures in the Far
West and how he had been twice bought--once with gold and once with
blood.

THE END.
